Foreword
This report has been conducted as a ﬁnal project of the Master of Science in Civil Enginnering
Degree, within the specialization of Building Structures, at The Technical University of Den-
marks’ Department of Civil Enginnering.
The thesis corresponds to ﬁve months of work, performed within the period from the 5th of
January 2015 to the 5th of June 2015, and is credited 30 ECTS points.
In addition to this report, a Experimental Report is also submitted as part of this thesis, which
is to be read in connection with this report.
The project has been carried out in cooperation with KHR Arkitekter, The Royal Danish
Academy of Fine Arts, School of Architecture and Fiberline Composites.

==PAGE:5==
Abstract
Glass ﬁbre reinforced polymer (GFRP) are increasingly winning impact in the building industry
and while particularly being used for fa¸cade elements and decking systems, its potential as a
structural material is being realised as its usage grows. However, structural design of such a
material is challenging as no commonly recognized rules and regulations exist, forcing producers
of structural proﬁles to issue design guides to assist designers. While these guides are a great
help, they are limited to the knowledge that exists and only applicable for regular structures
and the proﬁles of the producer.
This report is focused around a case structure, being a prototype for a potential building system
of GFRP. A structural analysis of parts of this case structure is performed in order to establish
a feasible and structurally capable rigid frame as a repeated element for the case structure. The
rigid frame is designed to be constructed either with bolted or adhesive connections. In order to
do this, existing guides and manuals of proﬁle design are studied, as well as theoretical research
of bolted and adhesive connections, in order to establish a safe design approach for the frame.
Whereas proﬁle and bolted connection designs are well established disciplines and adaptation
of the materials behaviour is the primary challenge, adhesive connections is a niche application
in civil engineering, with very limited guidance and reference work. To ensure a safe approach,
two independent methods are investigated and veriﬁed against a Finite Element Model and the
ﬁnal design is required to comply with both methods.
The goal of the analysis was not only to design a rigid frame purely of GFRP, but also to gather
experience about structural design of the material. It was found that the major challenge lies in
utilizing the strengths of the material, to avoid that low stiﬀness and long term shear capacity
becomes critical for the design. It is found that designing in order to fully utilize the high axial
strength of the material is hardly realistic for a frame structure. Connection design was likewise
found to be a critical aspect for the structure, but it was found that the capacity of bolted
connections can potentially be largely increased by including eﬀects of the anisotropic stiﬀness.
For the adhesive connection, it was found that one of the investigated approaches results in
much larger capacity than the other, also showing a potential for larger capacity veriﬁcations if
more detailed and broadly accepted approaches are developed. Suggestions for improvements of
the designed frames are given, as well as general recommendations for future structural design
of GFRP.
Parallel to the work of this report, experimental research was carried out as part of this thesis,
to verify the results and assumptions of the connection design from the analysis. Description
and results from this experimental work are found in the separate Experimental Report.

Acknowledgements
I would like to express my gratitude to all who have contributed to and been involved in the
work of this thesis, as well as all who have supported me and given inspiration and motivation
during the work. In particular I would like to thank the following people, without whom this
work could not have been done:
• Henrik Almegaard, for showing interest in supervising the project and given sound guid-
ance and advice. Thanks for keeping the project on track with a progressive focus.
• Jan Søndergaard, without whom the project would not have existed. Thanks for initiating
the project and for giving continuous input, challenges and inspiration. And thanks for
introducing me to many of the other people involved in the project and giving me insight
in the interest and development within this ﬁeld.
• Vicki Thake, for helping me understand the inherent architecture in the material and
its aesthetic potential. Thanks for the encouragement and the motivation given through
positivity and enthusiasm to the subject.
• Henrik Thorning, Stig Krogh Pedersen, Preben Venø Nielsen, Fritz Vinter and the rest
of Fiberline Composites, for supplying the test specimens and giving advice and under-
standing about the material and its production.
• The laboratory personal at DTU, for help and technical expertise in preparing and per-
forming the experimental work.

==PAGE:15==
Chapter 1
Introduction
Fibre reinforced polymers (FRP) are increasingly becoming implemented in structural engi-
neering world wide. While initially found appropriate predominantly for structures with special
requirements in regards to corrosion protection or electromagnetic transparency, it is starting
to be used actively as load bearing material in new buildings and bridges, as well as being
used for strengthening of existing structures, without these special circumstances being present.
As recognized structural norms and codes are being developed, together with improvements in
production methods, price, properties and availability, it can be predicted that FRP is about to
enter a stage, where it becomes regarded as a routine structural material, rather than a niche
application.
From a structural point of view, FRP possesses great potential, with a strength parallel to the
main ﬁbre direction that is comparable to that of steel, while having less than the quarter of the
density. However, due to relatively low stiﬀness and reduced strength in the other directions,
this potential is rarely utilized, as design becomes dominated by the weaknesses of the material
rather than the strengths. If FRP is to truly compete with conventional structural materials
and its potential be reached, traditions for how to design in order to utilize the strengths of
the material must be developed, together with recognized structural design codes to guide this
design.
This thesis seeks to contribute to the development of such a tradition and to identify where
there is a particular need for further development of existing guides and codes.
1
==PAGE:16==
Chapter 1. Introduction
2
1.1
Project purpose
The work of this thesis was initiated as part of an existing research project by KHR Arkitek-
ter and The Royal Danish Academy of Fine Arts, School of Architecture in cooperation with
Fiberline Composites. The objective of this research project is to illustrate the architectural
opportunities of FRP, and concretely develop a prototype for a possible future building sys-
tem, that is based upon the premisses and properties of FRP. The ambition of this prototype
building, and to which this thesis seeks to contribute, is to further enable the use of FRP as
an integrated construction material in the building sector. The architectural research wishes
to explore the inherent architecture of the material, which is deﬁned through the internal ﬁbre
composition. Changing the ﬁbre types and layout can thus give the desired aesthetic expres-
sions, but likewise changes the mechanical properties. Due to the complexity of the material
and the property variation being related to diﬀerent professions, development of the material is
dependent on close cooperation between architects and engineers.
The prototype building will be the case structure investigated in this report, allowing the struc-
tural considerations to be based around an actual structure, and these consideration to be
utilized in the ﬁnal structural design of the building. The work of this thesis has hereby been
part of a close multidisciplinary collaboration between engineering, architecture and production,
enjoying inputs from a broad diversity of skills and backgrounds.
1.2
Thesis structure
This report is built up starting with a theory chapter, which will present the mechanical prop-
erties of FRP, as well as existing calculation methods for the material together with the speciﬁc
approaches that will be used in the following analysis chapter. The analysis chapter will be
based around the case structure, and consist of a full structural design of a repeated frame,
used in the building. The results from the analysis chapter will be discussed in the following
discussion chapter after which conclusions will be made. Parallel to this report, an experimental
report is produced, which is to be read in connection to this report. In the experimental report
parts of the results from the analysis chapter is controlled in physical experiments. Practically,
this division into two reports is due to delays in regards to the initialisation of the experiments,
resulting in them ﬁrst being commenced shortly before submission of this thesis, and some of
the experiments still not being performed at the time of submission. The current state and
==PAGE:17==
Chapter 1. Introduction
3
planning of the experimental work is further explained in the experimental report, together
with the results from the experiments that were ﬁnalized on time for this submission.
1.3
Research question
Through the report, the thesis seeks to answer following research question:
What challenges exist when performing structural design of glass ﬁbre
reinforced polymer and which considerations must be taken into account in
order to develop the structural system that can be regarded as eﬃcient?
Here, as well as in the title of the thesis, the term eﬃcient structural system is to be understood
as a structural system that is not only feasible and capable of withstanding the external loads,
but also designed so that the capacity of all the structural elements are utilized, both in regards
to deﬂections, shear and axial stresses, as well as in the connection design.
1.4
Delimitations
This thesis does not intend to develop new in-depth theories concerning the mechanism within
the mechanical performance of FRP or the behaviour of bonding components. Instead it seeks
to clarify the extent of the knowledge available, the usability of existing knowledge in structural
design, as well as oﬀer guidance to what approaches to follow and considerations to take into
account when performing structural design of FRP structures and elements. Thus, this thesis is
limited to current approaches and existing theories available within the ﬁeld, and will be based
on these to oﬀer practical suggestion for the usage of them. In general the scope of this thesis
is subject to following delimitations:
• Only structural considerations are included (ﬁre safety, building energy, fa¸cade engineer-
ing, geotechnics, material degrading, building comfort etc. are not considered, though all
being relevant for the material and the case structure.)
• Instability of structural elements is only considered for column buckling, not including
lateral-torsional buckling or local plate buckling. This is due to the investigated structure
being restrained from these.
• Stresses due to thermal expansion and material creep is not included in the investigation.
==PAGE:18==
Chapter 2
Theory
In this chapter the properties of FRP will be presented with a particular focus on the mechanical
properties. Following this, existing design methods will be presented, to the extent that is used
in the analysis chapter. Finally, methods for designing and verifying the capacity of bolted and
adhesive connections will be presented, with a focus on the approach used in the analysis.
2.1
Reinforced polymer composites
FRP is a type of composite material, consisting of ﬁbres, used to resist tensile and compressive
loads, and a polymer matrix to transfer shear. The polymer matrix is typically polyester, vinyl
ester or epoxy, while the most common types of ﬁbres are glass ﬁbres, carbon ﬁbres and aramid
ﬁbres. Generally it can be said that carbon ﬁbres give high strength and stiﬀness, aramid ﬁbres
give high impact resistance, while glass ﬁbres give good all-round properties as well as electrical
insulation and electromagnetic transparency [D6]. Since the compressive and tensile strength
is predominantly governed by the ﬁbres, the orientation is essential. The ﬁbres are primarily
oriented in the longitudinal direction due to the manufacturing method, described in subsection
2.1.3. Due to the orientation of the ﬁbres the material is anisotropic, as can be seen in the
variation of the mechanical properties, which will be presented in subsection 2.1.1.
Apart from the mechanical properties, the material beneﬁts from being very resilient, with
excellent corrosion resistance and is generally resistant to a wide range of aggressive media,
making it suitable in harsh environments. Likewise it has electromagnetic neutrality, low axial
coeﬃcient of thermal expansion and low thermal conductivity, making it suitable for building
4
==PAGE:19==
Chapter 2. Theory
5
design. Furthermore, it is possible to form into complex shapes, which combined with a possible
transparent texture, potentially makes it an interesting architectural material. Weaknesses are
also related to the material, which, apart from a relatively high price, is dominated by a low
ﬁre resistance. While the material is not combustible itself, FRP in its basic form will degrade
at relatively low temperatures, resulting in diﬀusion of ﬂammable gasses and black smoke. This
can be limited by the use of ﬁre-retarding additives, added to the resin, greatly improving the
ﬁre resistance. However, a high smoke emission remains and ﬁre safety is deﬁnitely an important
topic to address in design of FRP, [A7], [A12].
Even though all the beneﬁts and disadvantages related to FRP are very interesting to explore
further, this report will focus entirely on the structural aspects of the material and its mechan-
ical behaviour and properties. These mechanical properties will be presented in the following
subsection.
2.1.1
Mechanical properties
The behaviour of FRP is linear elastic, as is seen in ﬁgure 2.1. The theoretically determined
curves, seen to the left (a), shows that the stiﬀness is dominated by the contribution of the
ﬁbres, which are perfectly linear elastic. However, the curve to the right (b), shows that in
the actual response of FRP, the material stiﬀens under increased tensile load and is thus not
perfectly linear elastic. This is due to the tensile load aligning lightly crimped ﬁbres, and will
therefore not be the case under compressive loading. Failure initiates with ﬁbres fracturing or
delaminating, resulting in drastically decreased stiﬀness until ultimate failure, [A12].
The mechanical properties of any reinforced polymer composite is largely dependent on the
choice of reinforcement and resin material, the ratio between reinforcement and resin quantity,
as well as the distribution and alignment of the ﬁbre layers. As mentioned above, the reinforcing
ﬁbres typically vary between glass, aramid and carbon while the resin can typically vary between
polyester, vinyl ester and epoxy, while many other reinforcing and matrix material can be
used. To give an indication of the mechanical performance of these diﬀerent materials, typical
mechanical properties are shown in table 2.1.
As is seen, the properties of the reinforcement vary signiﬁcantly, with glass ﬁbres having high
strength, carbon much higher stiﬀness and aramid low density. The matrix resins do not vary
as signiﬁcantly, but still to some extent. From these properties, it is obvious that the choice of
==PAGE:20==
Chapter 2. Theory
6

actual behaviour (from [A12]).

reinforcing ﬁbres, as well as matrix resin, will have a large impact on the mechanical properties of
the composite material. Even more signiﬁcantly, it is noticed how large an impact the quantity
ratio between reinforcing ﬁbres and matrix resin must play. To illustrate how these diﬀerent
element contribute to the overall mechanical behaviour of the material, indication can be found
from following formula from [A11], which oﬀers a guidance of the impact of the contribution of
the two components in a composite:
Xc = VrXr + VfXf
(2.1)
Where Xc is the property of the composite, Vr and Vf the volume of resin and ﬁbres, respectively,
and Xr and Xf being the resin and ﬁbre property. For example, a Fiberline proﬁle typically
has 60% ﬁbre content of glass ﬁbres by mass, according to [D6], and the remaining material
being polyester. Inserting this material content transferred to be by volume and the material
==PAGE:21==
Chapter 2. Theory
7
properties for glass ﬁbres and polyester, gives a tensile strength in the direction of the ﬁbres of:
ft,c
= 0.6 · 1130 kg/m3
2600 kg/m3 · 3450 MPa + 0.4 · 2600 kg/m3
1130 kg/m3 · 77 MPa
= 900 MPa + 71 MPa = 971 MPa
(2.2)
From this calculation, it is seen that almost the entire tensile strength is governed by the
reinforcing ﬁbres. However, the results of 971 MPa is of very poor precision, as it takes no
account of the placing, direction, alignment or continuity of the ﬁbres, but assumes it ideal, so
the full capacity is utilized, resulting in a much higher strength than what is practically possible.
Furthermore, such a composition would be undesirable, as it would oﬀer very poor transverse
properties. To have more balanced proﬁle, with also some poly-directional reinforcement, mats
of reinforcements are added to the reinforcement pack. A 3 mm plate proﬁle of GFRP would
thus typically consists of external plies, being a thin outer ﬁbre mat of about 0.5 mm, a layer
of roving, of about 2 mm and another external plies of about 0.5 mm. A layer of roving is
illustrated left in ﬁgure 2.2, while a layer of mats are illustrated to the right.
Figure 2.2: Fibre roving (left) and mat (right) (from [D6]).
According to [A11], a roving layer could have a reinforcement contents of about 65%, and a
tensile strength in the direction of the ﬁbres of about 800 MPa, being fairly close to the quantity
that would be achieved by using equation (2.1). A layer of mats will typically have a ﬁbre
content of about 25%, and a unidirectional tensile strength of only about 70 MPa, much lower
than what would be found by using equation (2.1), and often even lower than that of the resin
alone. However, the mats are neccessary to achieve the right constituency of the proﬁles. [A11]
suggests, that the mechanical properties of a GFRP proﬁle, can be indicated by the average of
the layer properties, relative to their thickness. The 3mm plate proﬁle mentioned above could
then be predicted to have a tensile strength parallel to the ﬁbres of the roving layer of 550 MPa.
This is still signiﬁcantly higher than the characteristic strength property usually used to describe
the mechanical properties of the material, but as the characteristic value is based on the lower
==PAGE:22==
Chapter 2. Theory
8
5% fractile, a tensile strength of 550 MPa parallel to the main ﬁbre direction could represent
the mean strength of the material. A thicker plate could have a more complex ﬁbre composition
containing thicker layers of mats with multiple ﬁbre orientations. The approach presented here
gives an insight of the impact of the combination of ﬁbres layers, and the high strength a roving
layer gives in the primary ﬁbre direction, explaining the high anisotropic nature of the material.
Using mats of ﬁbres, any ﬁbre orientation is potentially possible, resulting in ﬁbres oriented in
all directions, and it is therefore not correct to talk about an actual ﬁbres direction, as it is with
wood. Instead the strong direction of the material will be called the direction of pultrusion,
which the roving layers follow, making it the primary, but not sole, ﬁbre direction. In this
report, only GFRP will be considered, meaning purely polymer matrices reinforced with glass
ﬁbres. This is in spite of the improved mechanical properties that can be acquired by using
carbon ﬁbres. However, due to carbon ﬁbres being signiﬁcantly more expensive, they are, as the
market is now, not believed to be suitable as a general building material, though its relevance
could be found in special architectural features or high performance elements.
The overall mechanical properties of GFRP are given in table 2.2, which are based on proﬁles
from Fiberline and taken from [D6]. In this report, these mechanical properties for GFRP will
be used, even though many variations can be found, as the mechanical properties are highly
dependent on the used polymer matrix and layers of ﬁbres, that all must be carefully balanced
to obtain good mechanical properties.

From table 2.2 it is seen that the material has from half, down to almost a ﬁfth of the strength
perpendicular to the direction of pultrusion than parallel to the direction of pultrusion. It is
also noticed that the shear strength is as little as about a tenth of the ﬂexural, tensile and
compressive strength parallel to the direction of pultrusion. To have something to hold the
values from table 2.2 up against, they will in the following subsection be compared to other,
more commonly used, structural materials.
2.1.2
Comparison to other materials
To evaluate the behaviour of GFRP as a structural material, some of the structural properties
are shown in table 2.3 together with those of steel and wood. This is done to create a sense of the
approach by which the structural design shall be done, as steel and wood are considered to be
more common structural materials, with a more established design approach. In the table, the
yield strengths of steel is used, while the anisotropy of GFRP and wood is illustrated by giving
both the value for the strong and weak direction. It shall be noted, that the shown values are
characteristic values and due to diﬀerences in manufacturing and behaviour, the design value
are reduced diﬀerently by safety coeﬃcients for the diﬀerent materials.

From table 2.3 it is seen that the strength of GFRP is closer to that of steel than that of wood,
while the moduli are closer to those of wood. When comparing the mechanical properties to
those of steel, it is seen that while the tensile and compressive strength parallel to the direction of
pultrusion are slightly higher than that of steel, the shear strength is less than a ﬁfth, indicating
shear as a critical load. Examining the moduli, it is seen that even parallel to the direction of
pultrusion, GFRP has less than a seventh of the E-modulus of steel, while the shear modulus
is 1/27 times that of steel. Such a low shear modulus means that, unlike the case for steel,
==PAGE:24==
Chapter 2. Theory
10
GFRP should take shear deformations into account, which could have a impact in deﬂections.
Weighing less than a quarter of steel, GFRP has a much better strength to weight ratio, if based
on the high compressive and tensile strength parallel to the direction of pultrusion. However, as
this comparison has shown, the low weight does not out-weight the reductions in shear strength
or the moduli. Comparing the mechanical properties to that of wood, it is noticed that all
the strengths are at least 10 times as large for GFRP as those for wood, while the modulus of
elasticity is only 2.5 times as large. With the weight being about 4.5 times as large, it is again
seen that the strength to weight ratio is better for GFRP, while the E-modulus to weight ratio
is worse for GFRP than for wood.
In table 2.3 a price index value is also shown, taken from [B4]. This price indication must be
taken with caution, as prices can vary with time and location. Furthermore, it is given as the
price per kg, and must therefore be seen relative to the density. Taking the density diﬀerence
into account, it is noticed that the same proﬁle of GFRP and steel can be expected to have
around the same price, while a similar sized timber section will be signiﬁcantly cheaper. Based
on a purely economic and structural perspective, this means that GFRP can easily compete
with steel if axial stresses are dominant, as the price for the same proﬁle will be comparable but
the weight much lower. However, in most cases deﬂection or shear capacity will be dominant
for the GFRP proﬁle, compromising this competitive ability.
Based on this comparison, it is seen that GFRP has very good axial strength properties relative
to its weight compared to both steel and wood, while the moduli are low, strongly indicating
that conventional structural design would result in deﬂections being critical. It is also noticed
that the shear strength is relatively low compared to steel and the relation between the shear
strength and the axial strengths, is closer to that of wood. However, most GFRP proﬁles are
shaped similarly to those of steel, where the cross-section is optimized for moment of inertia,
while wood sections tend to be rectangular, leaving the entire area to transfer the shear. Based
on this, assuming the proﬁles to resemble steel sections, it can be expected that shear will be
the critical load.
2.1.3
Manufacturing by pultrusion
The continuous emergence of FRP as a structural material for building and civil structures is
largely possible due to the development of the pultrusion technique, which enables cost-eﬀective
production of high quality constant cross-section proﬁles. This technique has been developing
==PAGE:25==
Chapter 2. Theory
11
since the 1950’s, and has now reached a stage where it can produce structural elements, such
as beams and columns that can compete with those of conventional materials. As the demand
continuous to grow, the cost-eﬃciency could likewise be expected to be improved, as proﬁles to
a even further extent can be continuously produced, where as now, tools must be adjusted to
produce diﬀerent proﬁles dependent on the demand, [A1].
In this subsection, the manufacturing process of pultrusion will be described. This is reckoned
to be an essential topic to cover, as the manufacturing process to a large extent dictates the
possibilities of varying the structural elements, analysed within the scope of this thesis.
Pultrusion is a continuous fabrication method, where ﬁbres are spun through a guiding system
to be impregnated in a liquid matrix resin.
The reinforced matrix resin is gathered into a
preformed shape, roughly matching the desired proﬁle shape, after which it enters a heated
curing die. In the die, the pack has been changed into a fully shaped solid proﬁle. When cooled
suﬃciently, the proﬁle is pulled through a cutoﬀsaw, which cuts the proﬁle to the required
length. An overview of the production method is shown in ﬁgure 2.3. The production time is
about 1 m per minute. Generally pultrusion machines can fabric proﬁles with dimensions of up
to 1300 mm width, 350 mm height and a length, which often ranges from 12 m and up to above
18 m, [A11].
Figure 2.3: Overview of the pultrusion machine (from [D6]).
To obtain a high quality product, an optimum reinforcement to resin ratio must be used. Any use
of resin additives are introduced during the impregnation stage and these will aﬀect the optimum
reinforcement to resin ratio. The additives could be desired to reduce proﬁle cost, ﬁre retardancy
etc., [A11]. The resin can be added, either by using open resin wet-bath impregnation techniques
or by pressure injection of matrix resin directly into the die. The open wet-bath technique is
generally cheaper, while the impregnation can be favoured as it is better at retaining the required
multiaxial reinforcement and as it does not lose styrene to the surrounding factory environment.
The impregnation method also has advantages in regards to changing the production line from
==PAGE:26==
Chapter 2. Theory
12
one proﬁle or matrix to another, [A11]. The resin is added through the impregnation method
at the factory of Fiberline, which is illustrated in ﬁgure 2.4.
Figure 2.4: Illustration of impregnation method (from [A11]).
From the description of the production method, given above, the following observations that
are relevant for the proﬁle design can be noted:
• Due to continuous production method, proﬁles must have constant cross-section.
• Reinforcement will predominantly be aligned with the length of the proﬁles, while trans-
verse reinforcement can be obtained by the use of mats.
• The pultrusion machine sets certain limitations to the cross-sectional dimensions of the
proﬁles, while the length of the proﬁle can be very long.
• The amount of reinforcement in a proﬁle is limited by the desired optimum reinforcement
to resin ratio.
• Adding additives aﬀects the optimum reinforcement to resin ratio.
2.1.4
Design consideration
The material properties shows that GFRP is strong in the axial direction, parallel to the di-
rection of pultrusion, while it suﬀers from lower strengths in the other directions. Likewise the
shear stress is low in relation to the axial strength. The stiﬀness of the material is also low in
relation to the high strength parallel to the direction of pultrusion. As mentioned above, this
indicates that the material will be sensitive to shear loading and deﬂections. Based on these
considerations it is clear that the material has advantages in truss structures, where a large
height is obtained to give the neccessary inertia to outweigh the low stiﬀness. A truss structure
==PAGE:27==
Chapter 2. Theory
13
is also characterised by the members being axially loaded, utilizing the highest strength prop-
erties. However, in some structures a truss are not suitable, either due to the architecture, low
span length or limited available height. In such a case, tall proﬁles should be desired to gain
high modulus of inertia to avoid large deﬂections. While deﬂections will be reduced, so will
be axial stresses from the moment, where the material is strong. Therefore tall proﬁles alone
will not be able to utilize the properties of the material. To reduce deﬂection further, rigid
connections could be preferred, as these reduce the deﬂections to a ﬁfth compared to a simply
supported beam, while reducing the moment by a third. By combining rigid supports with tall
proﬁles, a better utilization of the properties could be obtained. This causes a new challenge,
being the design of rigid connections, which will result in connection forces perpendicular to the
direction of pultrusion occurring from the moment, where the material is weak.
In the following parts of this chapter, the general approach for verifying the bearing capacity
of elements of GFRP will be presented, followed by descriptions of methods to calculate rigid
connections of GFRP, both in regards to bolted and glued connections. Before this, a short
presentation of the lower bound theorem will be presented, which the design will be based on,
when uncertainties arise or simpliﬁcations must be made.
2.2
Lower Bound Theorem
Structural design of GFRP is a relatively new discipline and the established approaches and
calculation methods for exact solutions are limited. To make a safe design in spite of shortage
of thoroughly tested approaches, the philosophy of the Lower Bound Theorem can be used.
The lower bound theorem is part of the limit analysis approach of structural design, used
within the theory of plasticity. Opposed to the upper bound theorem, the lower bound theorem
is restrained to give only solutions which are on the safe side. To ensure this, the solution must
meet all statical and physical requirements.
To meet the statical requirements the element
or structure must be in equilibrium, both internally and externally. The internal equilibrium
means, that it must be theoretically possible to cut out any part of the element or structure
and require that this part is in equilibrium. The external equilibrium means, that the reactions
and external load must be in equilibrium. If these requirements are fulﬁlled for a chosen force
distribution, that force distribution can be said to be admissible and the requirements of the
lower bound theorem is met. This allows inﬁnitely many force distributions to be allowed, giving
the designer the possibility to go ahead with the design, based on a guessed force distribution.
==PAGE:28==
Chapter 2. Theory
14
As the distribution is more or less chosen freely, while fulﬁlling the requirements of admissibility,
it is likely that it does not represent the true force distribution that will occur in the structure.
The principle is, that this is not a problem in regards to safety, as the forces in reality will
follow the optimum, and thus the designers guess can only be a safe side estimate of the true
distribution, [A9]. Furthermore, the solution might be ensured to be even more on the safe side
by ignoring certain parameters or eﬀects, that would contribute to the load bearing capacity.
While the behaviour of the material is not plastic, but rather dominated by the elastic behaviour
of the ﬁbres, the design philosophy behind the lower bound method is found relevant to use
consciously in the structural design of this relatively new material. As a result of this design
philosophy, the solutions that are presented in the analysis chapter, will not necessarily be
optimal, but they can be expected to be safe. This is seen as a ﬁrst essential step in developing
eﬃcient structural design with the material, while optimal design is a further step for increasing
this eﬃciency.
2.3
Structural design with GFRP
As neither the Eurocode system nor other oﬃcially recognized codes or standards used in Den-
mark or internationally covers structural design with GFRP, a major part of the scope of this
thesis, is to explore the manuals and design guides that exist, evaluate these and determine
their usability on a predetermined structural design case. To assist and guide the designer, the
producers of GFRP elements publish their own manuals and design guides. This is also the case
for Fiberline Composites, who have based their design manual ([D6]) on the Eurocomp Design
Code ([D5]). In this section, the design approach presented in Fiberline’s design manual will
be presented, to the extent relevant to the analysis performed in the work of this report. The
procedure will be examined, to determine the approach on which it is based, with reference to
other sources. Where guidance is not available in Fiberline’s design manual or the detail level
inadequate for the analysis performed during the work of this thesis, theories from Eurocomp
and other guides, books and research papers are used.
2.3.1
Safety concept and coeﬃcients
In this subsection, the safety concept and safety coeﬃcients applied for structural design of
GFRP proﬁles is presented in accordance with [D6]. While the load combination concept is
==PAGE:29==
Chapter 2. Theory
15
very similar to that in the Eurocode system, and will not be elaborated much upon, the partial
safety coeﬃcients reducing the strengths of the material, will be linked to some studied theory.
These partial safety coeﬃcients, have an interesting element of having a signiﬁcantly larger
strength reduction for long term loading in comparison to short term loading. The reason for
this will be explained, based on literature studies of the material.
Load combination
Following the principles from the Eurocode system, [D6] operates with ultimate limit state
(ULS) for verifying bearing capacity and failure resistance and serviceability limit state (SLS)
to ensure acceptable deﬂections. In the following, the combination method given in [D6] will
be presented, to the extent used for the analysis in this report. This combination method is
identical to the one presented in [D5], which [D6] is based upon.
Ultimate limit state
For capacity veriﬁcation, both variable and permanent loads are signiﬁcantly increased. For load
cases with only one variable load, this is further increased and the design load combinations is
determined by:
Sd,k =
X
j=1,...,m
γG,jGk,j + 1.50Qk
(2.3)
Here γG,j is the partial coeﬃcient of permanent load (no. j), set to 1.35, or 1.0 in case the load
is stabilising, Gk,j is the permanent loads and Qk is the variable load. If several variable loads
are present, these are identically increased and the load combination is done by:
Sd,k =
X
j=1,...,m
γG,jGk,j + 1.35
X
j=1,...,m
Qk,i
(2.4)
This load combination from [D6] will generally results in larger design load combinations than if
based on the Eurocode procedure. This is especially in regards to the permanent load actions,
which are multiplied by a factor 1.35, where it according Eurocode ([D1] and [D3]), will be
increased by no more than a factor 1.2 for normal consequence class. The increase of a single
variable load corresponds with that of Eurocode, while for several variable loads it is somewhat
diﬀerent. While Eurocode operate with an dominant load, that is intensiﬁed as a single load in
equation (2.3), and reduction of the none-dominant loads, [D6] operates with an equal intensity
==PAGE:30==
Chapter 2. Theory
16
of all the variable loads, as shown in equation (2.4). Generally it is expected that the procedure
presented here, in accordance with [D6], will results in slightly larger combined load actions,
than if based on the Eurocode procedure, especially due to the increase of the permanent actions,
which are of particular relevance, as these must be taken in regards to the largely reduced long
term strength of the material.
Serviceability limit state
While it was found that the ULS combinations was signiﬁcantly diﬀerent from that of Eurocode,
the SLS combination can be used as in Eurocodes. [D6], formulates the SLS combination by:
SK,k = Gp,1 + Gp,2 + 1.00Qp,1 + ψL,2Qp,2 + ψL,3Qp,3
(2.5)
Where the ψ-factor is suggested as 0.9 according to [D5]. This value is also suggested in [D6],
if a more accurate value is not available and references are made to Eurocode as an alternative.
Here, the values of the ψ-factors given in Eurocode ([D3]), are regarded as more accurate, as
it takes the nature of the load action into account. For the analysis of this report the ψ-factor
from Eurocode ([D3]) will therefore be used. It is noticed that using the factor suggested in [D6]
is conservative, as none of the ψ-factor from Eurocode ([D3]), are as large as 0.9. Therefore,
using 0.9 will set harder requirements on the deﬂection of a structure, which is already expected
to be critical due to the low E-modulus, as mentioned in section 2.1. As in Eurocode, no actual
deformation limit is given in [D6], but between 1/200 to 1/400 of the span length is given as an
suggestion.
Safety factors
Here the partial safety coeﬃcients used for GFRP members will be presented, as given in [D6]
and discussed based on the theory behind the behaviour of the material under long and short
term loading.
According to [D6], the capacities of the material are to be reduced by:
Rd =
Rk
γm,1 γm,2 γm,3 γm,4
(2.6)
==PAGE:31==
Chapter 2. Theory
17
Where γm,1, γm,2 and γm,3 are determined in accordance with [D5] based on the production
methods of Fiberline, giving:
• γm,1=1.15, due to the proﬁles being pultruded
• γm,2=1.1, as the proﬁles are fully postcured
• γm,3=1.0, as the proﬁles have an ultimate heat distortion temperature for dimensional
stability of 100◦C.
These three partial coeﬃcients are determined based on table values from [D5], in correspon-
dence with the reasons stated above.
γm,4 is dependent on the operating temperature and load duration, and is in [D6] recommended
as γm,4 = 1.0 for short-term loads and γm,4 = 2.5 for long-term loads, for operation temperature
below 80◦C. These values are likewise based on values given in [D5]. This gives following total
safety coeﬃcients:
• Short-term load: γm = 1.3
• Long-term load: γm = 3.2
While all the partial safety coeﬃcient are based upon values from [D5], the combined safety
coeﬃcient for short-term loading, conﬂicts with the recommendations from [D5], which states
that γm should not be taken as less than 1.5 for building structures.
This view is shared
with [A12], stating that: 1.5 ≤γm ≤10. However, [D5], does also state, that more detailed
information and test data may justify a lesser value. Therefore, this is not to be seen as a
criticism of the safety approach from [D6], but rather as a indication that the safety coeﬃcient
γm = 1.3 for short-term loading, is low for the material in general, but assumed reasonable, as
purely based on values from [D5], and thus simply due to a precise fabrication process. The
greatly reduced strength in regards to long-term loading is due to the phenomenon; creep rupture
or static failure. This phenomenon can cause elements to fail at stresses signiﬁcantly lower than
their short-term capacity, due to sustained load actions, and is particularly pronoun for GFRP
elements, [A1], [B4]. The process is due to a formation of pores and microcracks forming at
grain boundaries, creating local stress concentrations, ultimately resulting in failure below the
general capacity of the material. The higher the temperature and the longer the duration of the
==PAGE:32==
Chapter 2. Theory
18
loading, the lower stress can cause fracture due to creep rupture, [A4]. This also explains, why
the long term loading is further reduced in case of operating temperatures above 80◦C in [D6].
The eﬀect of the two diﬀerent safety coeﬃcients dependent on the duration of the load, is
that load combinations must be split into short- and long-term actions, where long-term loads
according to [D6], are with a duration measured in years.
2.3.2
Design of structural members
In this subsection a description of the structural design of GFRP members will be presented.
Apart from a mere presentation of the approach given in Fiberline’s design manual, [D6], some
general theoretical consideration will also be given, which are used to evaluate the presented
approach. The structural member design presented here is that for beams and columns.
Beams
As mentioned in subsection 2.1.1, GFRP has a low shear modulus compared to steel, even when
viewed relatively to the E-modulus. Therefore basing the beam deﬂections on a Euler-Bernoulli
beam theory, will result in underestimating the deﬂection for short beams, as shear deformations
will play a signiﬁcant role. Therefore beam defection design should be carried out as Timoshenko
beams, where the cross-section plane no longer is assumed to remain perpendicular and shear
deformations are included, [A8]. The eﬀect of the shear deformation on a GFRP beam, as
a function of the span length is illustrated in [C3] and here shown in ﬁgure 2.5. The ﬁgure
shows the evenly distributed load that a 300 x 150 mm I-beam can sustain, before reaching
the deﬂection limits. According to [A8], the assumption of small shear deformations related to
the Euler-Bernoulli beam theory is generally true for beams with span/depth≥10. Based on
the plot of ﬁgure 2.5, this is clearly not true for GFRP beams, where there is more than 25%
larger capacity in regards to deﬂection if shear deformations are ignored, when span/depth=10.
According to [C3], as a general rule of thumb, the shear deformation of GFRP beam can ﬁrst
be ignored when span/depth>25, which corresponds with ﬁgure 2.5.
According to [D6], the shear deformation is taken into account, as the deformation limit is
controlled by:
Max(δ)

and [C3], often found to be the dimensioning for the design of a beam of GFRP.

by determining the largest moment and dividing this with the section modulus, W, by:

The k-values in equation (2.9) and (2.10) are, as for the deformations, factors given in [D6],

from [A3].
The shear area Ak is given for all Fiberline’s structural proﬁles, and seems to
be determined as the web thickness multiplied with the web height, to which half the ﬂange
thickness at times are added, as illustrated in ﬁgure 2.6.
Figure 2.6: Estimated shear area (from [C3]).
This method is also mentioned in [A6], which refers to the approach as an often accepted
simpliﬁcation. The precision of this formulation is increased the more the ﬂange area exceeds
the web area, for equal ﬂange and web area, a underestimation of 7% is expected, when compared
to shear stresses found by Grashof’s formula, which is suggested for both steel calculation in [A6]
and timber calculation in [A10]. According to Grashof’s formular, the shear stress is determined
by:
τ = Vz · ∆Sy
Iy · t
(2.11)
With Sy being the statical moment, Vz the shear force, Iy the moment of inertia and t the
thickness at the location where the shear stress is determined.
The approach of [D6] corresponds to the one given in [D5], which also oﬀers suggestions for
determining the shear area for standard proﬁle shapes.
In the analysis of this report, the largest shear force will be found both by use of a shear area, as
in [D6], and by Grashof’s formula. This is both to evaluate the method from [D6], but as much
due to the sections used in the scope of the report being composites of several standard proﬁles
from Fiberline and thus a table value is not available for these proﬁles. Likewise the proﬁles are
not standard proﬁles, so the recommendation of [D5] for determining the shear area cannot be
used. Furthermore, the ﬂange area of the top ﬂange is very small compared to the web area in
==PAGE:35==
Chapter 2. Theory
21
the proﬁles used in the analysis, which according to [A6] will give increased underestimation by
basing the shear stress on the shear area.
It shall be noted that no requirement of combination of the bending stress and shear stress is
mentioned in [D6]. While the maximum bending and shear stresses do not occur in the same
location, it could be argued that it should be controlled whether combinations between meeting
of web and ﬂange in the case of I-proﬁles should be controlled. Also, stability of beams are not
considered in [D6], such as lateral torsional buckling. Guidance for stability is oﬀered in [D5],
but as the beams in the analysis of this report will be supported against out-of-plane stability,
the methods will not be presented here.
Columns

capacity is suﬃcient if the design value of the normal force is smaller than critical column load,
determined by:

In this section a calculation method for bearing capacity veriﬁcation of GFRP element has
been presented and reﬂected upon. The following two sections will present some theories and
approaches to evaluate the bearing capacity and force distribution in rigid connection of GFRP
elements. This will be done for both bolted connections and adhesive joints.
==PAGE:37==
Chapter 2. Theory
23
2.4
Bolted connection design
According to [A5], a designer has two basic techniques available for joining components of
FRP, being mechanical fastening and adhesive bonding. In this section mechanical fastening
connections will be examined, speciﬁcally rigid bolted connections. Adhesive connection will
then follow in section 2.5. [A5] summarised the advantages and disadvantages of mechanical
fastened connection, in contrast to adhesive joint by:
Advantages:
• No surface preparation of components required.
• Disassembly possible without component damage.
• No abnormal inspection problems.
Disadvantages:
• Holes cause unavoidable stress concentrations.
• Can incur a large weight penalty
As seen from this list, the advantages by using a mechanically fastened connection are all related
to the construction and control of the connection, being signiﬁcantly simpler than an adhesive
joint, especially when done on site. The disadvantages are on the other hand related to more
structural consideration, as the bolted connection will create stress concentrations around the
holes and from the bolts. Despite the structural disadvantages, bolted connections are the most
common connection type in GFRP, [A5]. The objective in this section, is to present an approach
to calculate the bolt force distribution of a rigid bolted connection of GFRP and verifying the
capacity of the connection based on the distribution. Little guidance is found for design of
rigid connections of GFRP, as they are generally very scarcely used. Some researchers believe
that the low stiﬀness of pultruded proﬁles results in fully rigid connections not appearing to be
realistic, but will to some degree act as semirigid connection, [A1]. While keeping this aspect
in mind, the approach presented in this section, will be based upon an assumption of fully rigid
behaviour. It shall also be noted, that while bolted connections in steel can dissipate some of
the high peak stresses that occur against the edge of the holes through local yielding, GFRP has
a linear elastic response almost until failure, resulting in an increased risk of local crushing at
==PAGE:38==
Chapter 2. Theory
24
the bolt holes. Because of this diﬀerence in behaviour from steel and the orthotropic properties
of the material, the traditions for bolted connection of steel cannot be directly transferred to
GFRP if the properties are to be utilized.
In the following subsection, diﬀerent approaches for the distribution of the bolt forces will
be presented, using diﬀerent detail level concerning the eﬀect of the orthotropic stiﬀness of
the material. These distributions will result in force components perpendicular and parallel
to the direction of pultrusion of the material, which can be held directly against capacities
determined based on an approach from Fiberline’s design guide, [D6]. This approach of capacity
determination, as well as the related requirements, are presented in subsection 2.4.2.
2.4.1
Force distribution
The force distribution within a rigid, moment transferring connection will here be presented.
First, an approach for the moment distribution will be presented and illustrated that disregards
the eﬀect of the orthotropic stiﬀness of the material. Following this, two suggestions for including
this inﬂuence of the stiﬀness is presented, where one conservatively simpliﬁes the distribution
by only distributing the forces parallel to the direction of pultrusion, while the other is based on
the stiﬀness ratio parallel and perpendicular to the direction of pultrusion. It will be expected
that the true force distribution will be strongly inﬂuenced by the variations of stiﬀness in the
material, as is the case for timber connection, which likewise has orthotropic stiﬀnesses. The
tendency of the forces to orient towards the higher stiﬀness is for example shown in [C2].
The diﬀerent approaches will be illustrated through the simple connection of a GFRP member
bolted to an isotropic gusset plate, as shown on ﬁgure 2.8. All the approaches will be based
upon a distribution using the elasticity theory, as they shall according to [D6].
Disregarding the variations in stiﬀness, the distribution of the moment results in bolt forces
proportional to the distance from the centroid of the connection to the individual bolts. The
force from the moment in each bolt can, according to an approach for timber structures given
in [A10], be determined by:
Fi,m,d = Md

Based on the resulting forces illustrated left in ﬁgure 2.10, a horizontal and vertical component
can be determined, as illustrated to the right of ﬁgure 2.10. The force components can then be
used to check the combined capacity in relation to the capacities parallel and perpendicular to
the direction of pultrusion.
As mentioned, this approach disregards the diﬀerence in E-moduli parallel and perpendicular
to the direction of pultrusion, which in reality will aﬀect the forces, as the higher stiﬀness will
attract larger load. Therefore it can be conceived to be somewhat imprecise, and presumably
conservative, as it distributes the forces equally in the strong and weak direction in the material,
resulting in over-utilization perpendicular to the direction of pultrusion. If the connection on
the other hand had been between two identical GFRP elements rotated 90 degrees relative to
each other, the orthotropic stiﬀness of the two elements would cancel out each other in regards
to the bolt force distribution, making disregarding the E-moduli appropriate. In the case here,
where there is more stiﬀness and strength in one direction than the other, it is of interest to
make a more detailed model, which includes the eﬀect of the stiﬀness, possibly giving more
precise results, resulting in a larger capacity. The eﬀect of the varying stiﬀness can be taken
into account in an exaggerated manner, which will be easy to calculate. This can be done by
distributing the forces from the moment purely in the direction of pultrusion, disregarding any
capacity in the direction perpendicular to the direction of pultrusion. This case is considered,
where the entire moment is taken in the bolt as either vertical or horizontal forces. This is done
similarly to equation (2.18), but using only the horizontal distance, x or the vertical distance,

distribution are larger than when disregarding the E-moduli, as the moment arm is shorter.
However, as the material has more than twice the capacity parallel to the direction of pultrusion,
it is not necessarily more conservative. The shear force will be added, as when disregarding the
moduli of elasticity, creating some forces perpendicular to the direction of pultrusion.

One could argue that all the distributions are feasible, as they are all admissible and in equi-
librium with the external forces and follow the requirements of the lower bond theorem, and
strictly speaking they could all represent the true distribution, though the one dependent on the
ratio of the E-moduli is expected to be closest to the truth. During the analysis in chapter 3 of
this report, design will be carried out based on all the three diﬀerent approaches. Thus a force
distribution will be performed based on the rigid force distribution using equation (2.18) and
the approach dependent on the ratio of moduli of elasticity, using equation (2.21), as well as a
distribution purely in the direction of the largest stiﬀness according to equation (2.20). Hereby,
the analysis will give an indication of which distributions are most beneﬁcial and to what extent
the results vary from each other. The forces will in reality seek toward a distribution which gives
the highest capacity, and thus the distribution being most beneﬁcial is also likely to be closest
to the true distribution. The calculations are performed in MatLab using the script shown in
Appendix A. To insert the coordinates of the bolts in the connection in the MatLab-script, the
’lisp-code’ shown in Appendix B is used, whereby the coordinates of the bolts from a connection
drawn in AutoCAD are automatically obtained.

2.4.2
Capacity determination
In the previous subsection, approaches were given to determine the bolt forces in a rigid con-
nection, which are admissible and ensure equilibrium with the external forces. Thus the ﬁrst of
three criteria giving in Fiberline’s design manual, [D6], for bolted connection design are followed.
The criteria given are:
• The required bolt forces are suﬃcient to reach equilibrium with the existing shear forces.
• The shear forces for which the bolted joint is dimensioned, must be transferred by laminate
in the remaining surface between the bolt group and the rest of the proﬁle; i.e. it must
not be possible for the entire bolted joint to tear out the proﬁle.
• The contact compression between the proﬁle must be able to be absorbed locally.
The remaining two criteria relate to the local failure modes within the bolt group. These failure
modes are studies in [D6] and all evaluated relative to each other, both parallel and perpendicular
to the direction of pultrusion. These evaluations are performed under the assumption of given
minimum distances, which are shown in ﬁgure 2.13.
Figure 2.13: Minimum distances for bolted connection (from [D6]).
The failure modes considered are shown in ﬁgure 2.14 to 2.16, for both load parallel to the
direction of pultrusion and perpendicular to the direction of pultrusion. The derivation of the
capacity of the diﬀerent failure modes will not be given here, instead reference is made to [D6].
The critical failure modes are found to be failure mode 3 (ﬁgure 2.15, left) for load parallel to
the direction of pultrusion, giving following capacity:
PB,D,0◦= d t 150 MPa
γm
(2.22)
==PAGE:44==
Chapter 2. Theory
30
Where d is the bolt diameter, t is the laminate thickness and γm = 1.3 is the safety coeﬃcient.
For load perpendicular to the direction of pultrusion, failure mode 5 (ﬁgure 2.16) is most critical.
This gives following capacity:
PB,D,90◦= d t 70 MPa
γm
(2.23)
It is seen that the capacities are directly proportional to both the bolt size and the proﬁle
thickness. If a bolt is loaded both parallel and perpendicular to the direction of pultrusion, the
combined load-bearing capacity is checked to be suﬃcient by following criterion:


A condition for using this procedure is that no more than 4 bolts are placed in a row. Further-
more it shall be controlled that the hole reduced cross section of the GFRP proﬁle has suﬃcient
capacity to transfer the loads. As seen, the strength parallel to the direction of pultrusion are
more than double that perpendicular to the direction of pultrusion. Seen in relation the force
distribution dependent to the stiﬀness, this shows a potentially great advantage of taking the
increased force in the direction with high stiﬀness into account, in order to utilize the increased
strength in this direction.

2.5
Adhesive joints
In the previous section, an approach for the capacity veriﬁcation of rigid bolted connections was
presented. It was found that distributing the forces based on the E-moduli of the orthotropic
material, could reduce the forces in the weak direction perpendicular to the direction of pul-
trusion. This could be advantageous in regards to give a more precise result, which also results
in a veriﬁcation of a higher capacity. Despite of this, a bolted connection will always create
highly concentrated forces in a rigid connection, which will partly be directed perpendicular to
the direction of pultrusion. Another option of joining the pultruded GFRP proﬁles is through
adhesive connections, where the main advantages and disadvantages are summarised by [A5],
in relation to mechanically fastened joints by:
Advantages:
• Stress concentration can be minimised.
==PAGE:46==
Chapter 2. Theory
32
• Incur a small weight penalty.
Disadvantages:
• Disassembly impossible without component damage.
• Can be severely weakened by environmental eﬀects.
• Require surface preparation.
• Integrity diﬃcult to conﬁrm by inspection.
In contrast to the bolted connections, the advantages here are structural, while the disadvan-
tages are related to the assembly and construction process. Probably due to a limited trust to
the capacity and diﬃculties in controlling the quality of the connection, adhesive joints are only
used to a very limited extent for primary structural elements. If used, it is mainly to improve
serviceability performance and combined with mechanical fasteners to transfer the loads in fail-
ure situations, [A1]. This section will present methods to determine the capacity of an adhesive
connection, with the objective of understanding the behaviour of an adhesive connection and
discover the parameters of importance for the bearing capacity. Generally, analytical methods
for calculating adhesive connections have predominantly been developed for single and double
lap joints.
In an adhesive connection, the tension, compression and shear in the connected elements will
be transferred as shear through the adhesive. If the connection is assumed inﬁnitely rigid, as
in ﬁgure 2.17a, the shear force can simply be determined by dividing the transferred load with
the area of the adhesive layer:
τm = P
bl
(2.25)
With b being the width of the adhesive layer and l being the length.
However, as both the GFRP elements, also called the adherents in this context, and the adhesive
are not completely rigid, but elastic, a more complex stress distribution, as shown in ﬁgure 2.17b,
must be considered. The stresses occurring in the connection can result in several failure modes,
being either in the bonding between the adhesive and the adherent, within the adhesive or in
the adherent, through tearing or breaking. The diﬀerent failure modes are illustrated in ﬁgure
2.18. In this report, distinction will not be made between failure in the bonding or within the
adhesive layer (called cohesive failure in the ﬁgure), as all failure due to the capacity of the
==PAGE:47==
Chapter 2. Theory
33
Figure 2.17: Exaggerated deformation of adhesive joint and shear distribution a) with rigid
adherent, b) with elastic adherent (from [A5]).
glue will be referred to as adhesive failure. Failure of the GFRP elements are only treated due
to tearing in the connection design of the adhesive connections, as the breaking of the entire
element, is controlled in the proﬁle design.
Figure 2.18: Failure modes of adhesive joints (from [A5]).
In a single lap joint, as the one illustration in ﬁgure 2.17 and 2.18, the stress distribution will
be further complicated due to the asymmetry creating a bending moment and rotation in the
joint, causing non-linear problems, [A5]. These complication will not occur to the same extent
in a double lap joint, as it is more symmetric. A typical double lap joint is shown in ﬁgure 2.19.
This kind of joint will best represent the connections analysed in this report, which can be
simpliﬁed into multiple double lap joints and the capacity thus veriﬁed. Therefore, this section
==PAGE:48==
Chapter 2. Theory
34
Figure 2.19: Notation for double lap joint (From [D5]).
will focus on double lap joints and the analytical methods for determining the stress distribution
will be presented for such a joint. The determination of the stresses and the veriﬁcation of the
capacity will be determined based on two diﬀerent approaches from two diﬀerent sources. The
ﬁrst approach is based on the Eurocomp Design Code and Handbook, [D5], being among the
most recognized guides for FRP structures. This approach assumes failure in the adhesive layer
due to shear or out-of-plane tension, or purely due to out-of-plane tension in the GFRP plates.
However, the approach veriﬁes the capacities separately with no interaction between the stresses.
The second approach, based on [C4], assumes failure in the GFRP proﬁle due to delamination
between the diﬀerent layers of ﬁbres through combined shear and out-of-plane tension. This
is found to be the dominant failure mode, based on experimental research. Hereby the second
approach disregards failure in the adhesive, which is the main failure in the ﬁrst approach. The
two approaches are hereby conﬂicting and both will be investigated in this report. As the two
approaches are unrelated and conﬂicting, it is important to distinguish them from each other,
and they will therefore be presented separately in the following. After both approaches are
presented, they will be discussed and compared.
Several parameters are included in this presentation of the approaches, and to give an under-
standing of the magnitude of these parameters, typical values are presented in table 2.4.

2.5.1
Approach 1: Eurocomp Design Code and Handbook
In [D5], an approach for determining the maximum shear stress in a double lap joint is suggested,
developed by Hart-Smith and initially presented in [B3]. This approach uses an elastic-plastic
representation, rather than the purely rigid or elastic representation. The approach is based on
5 steps, which will be summarized in the following. The central parameters are illustrated in
ﬁgure 2.19.

be in [m−1] and thus the thicknesses must be inserted in [m].

==PAGE:52==
Chapter 2. Theory
38
2.5.2
Approach 2: Vall´ee, Adhesive Bonded Lap Joints of Pultruded GFRP
Shapes
The second approach is presented as a result of comprehensive physical and numerical exper-
iments, as part of a Ph.D. thesis by Till Vall´ee, [C4]. Here the approach from the Eurocomp
Design Code and Handbook ([D5]) is criticized for not including interaction formulas, and for
not being fully adapted to use for adhesive bonding of pultruded proﬁles, as failure is assumed
in the adhesive layer, or in the material under one individual stress component, rather than a
combination. Furthermore, it claims that the mathematical formulations are not adapted to
civil engineering application, due to them being based on small sized samples. Despite this
criticism, the approach from [D5] is found relevant to present and to use in the analysis, as
this is a commonly recognized design guide and the one which Fiberline’s design manual ([D6])
is based upon. However, the criticism from [C4] gives rise to regarding the veriﬁcations done
according to [D5] with caution.
Instead [C4] suggests a diﬀerent approach, which is based on a failure mode, as shown in ﬁgure
2.21, which was found to be the only occurring failure mode in both physical and numerical
experiments. This failure invokes peeling of a laminate of the pultruded proﬁle, e.i. neither
based on the tensile strength parallel or perpendicular to the main ﬁbre direction, but the
tensile strength between two layers of ﬁbre rovings or mats. The study done in [C4] is partially
analytical, numerical and experimental. The research is based on Fiberline proﬁles, so results
should be adaptable to the work of this report. As no out-of-plane tensile strength is given for
Fiberline’s proﬁle in the design manual, it is determined by experiments in [C4], and found to
be as shown in table 2.4.
Figure 2.21: Failure mode of double lap joint (From [C4]).
==PAGE:53==
Chapter 2. Theory
39
The approach for determining the stresses in [C4] is based on a method ﬁrst presented in [B2],
which is an extension and intended as an improvement of the method from [B3], which the ﬁrst
approach was based on. Like the ﬁrst approach, this method is based on a determination of the
shear stress within the adhesive layer. However, while both [B2] and [D5] use the thickness of
adhesive layer as a parameter, [C4] replaces this with rf, being the radius of the adhesive ﬁllet.
Apart from replacing the thickness with the radius of the ﬁllet, the determination of the shear
stress is identical with that in [B2]. The reason for using the ﬁllet radius, is that this was found
to have a stronger impact on the stress peak than the thickness. One could however argue, that
while it is very diﬃcult to practically control the thickness of the adhesive layer, controlling
the radius of the ﬁllet is practically impossible. Furthermore, one could expect that the radius
of the ﬁllet, would be dependent on the thickness in combination with parameters such as the
viscosity. In veriﬁcation of the analytical model [C4] keeps the adhesive thickness and radius
ﬁllet equal, again indicating the strong relation between the two parameters. The largest shear
stress is determined by:
τxz(x) = A sinh(βx) + B cosh(βx)
(2.38)
Where x is the location along the length of the connection, with x = 0 being the centre. β
is dependent on a relation between the shear modulus G and the E-moduli, as well as the
thicknesses and ﬁllet radius. The relation is given by:

2.5.3

the two methods are hereby isolated to this single factor depending on the shear moduli and the
thicknesses. For typical double lap joint with typical material properties, the diﬀerence between
the value of λ in the ﬁrst approach and β in the second will be between a factor of 1.25 and 2.0.
As the shear stress is directly proportional with this factor, the signiﬁcance is large, resulting
in a higher shear stress when using the ﬁrst approach.
Both approaches ﬁnd the maximum out-of-plane stress based on this maximum shear stress.
This means that the diﬀerence of the prediction of the shear stress likewise aﬀects the estimation
==PAGE:57==
Chapter 2. Theory
43
of the out-of-plane stress. Comparing the two equations of determining the out-of-plane stress,
from equation (2.32) and (2.43), it is seen that the two equations are almost identical. If the
ﬁllet radius, rf, is assumed to be equal to the adhesive thickness ,ta, and that the E-modulus of
the adhesive, Ea, is identical to the eﬀective modulus, E′
a, the determination of the maximum
out-of-plane stress from the shear stress will be the same. However, the eﬀective E-modulus,
E′
a, used in the ﬁrst approach is far from equal to the actual E-modulus of the adhesive. With
the properties of the adhesive used in the analysis, the eﬀective E-modulus is only about a ﬁfth
of the actual E-modulus. As the out-of-plane stress is directly proportional with this fourth
root of the E-modulus, this results in a reduction of the out-of-plane stress of a factor of about
0.7. This results in the ﬁrst approach estimates are higher shear stress, but a lower out-of-plane
stress, relative to the shear stress.
Based on these observations and rewritings of the equations, it is seen that the determination of
the maximum shear stress and maximum out-of-plane stress are eﬀectively very similar, though
seeming very diﬀerent at ﬁrst. However, the diﬀerences that exist between the two approaches
results in signiﬁcant variations in the prediction of the stresses.
The capacity determination is based on very diﬀerent principles, where the ﬁrst approach pre-
dominantly operates with failure in the adhesive, checking the shear and tensile capacity of
the adhesive separately, without interaction between the two stresses. The out-of-plane tensile
stress is also controlled for the GFRP members, but no shear inclusion is given for these. The
second approach on the other hand intentionally disregards failure in the adhesive, as the re-
search shows that failure will always happen due to delamination of the GFRP elements. Based
on the stresses in the adhesive, the shear and out-of-plane stresses in the GFRP elements are
determined and the capacity controlled through interaction formulas between the two stress
components. In the capacity veriﬁcation the ﬁrst approach uses the material strengths directly,
while the second approach found it appropriate to increase this strength. The argument for
this, was that the material strengths are determined for small specimens with constant stress
distribution. On the other hand, the behaviour of adhesive joint result in sharp stress peaks,
where the maximum stress is found. To make the analytical results correspond with the exper-
imental, it was found appropriate to increase the strength with a κ-factor dependent on the lap
length and chamfering of the joint, having an impact on the degree of the stress peak.
To create more in-depth and practical understanding of the signiﬁcance of the diﬀerences be-
tween the two approaches, both will be used in the analysis chapter of this report. As it is
==PAGE:58==
Chapter 2. Theory
44
understood, that the diﬀerences between the two approaches lie in two diﬀerent areas, being
the stress determination and the capacity veriﬁcation, these two areas will to some extent be
investigated separately. This is done, by initialising the analysis of the adhesive connection with
a method veriﬁcation of the stress determinations by comparison to Finite Element Analysis
(FEA). Through this comparison, the approaches are evaluated and the one showing best com-
pliance to the FEA will be used in the further connection design. In the connection design, the
capacity will be veriﬁed using both the approaches, thus checking the capacity of the adhesive
and the adherent. As the approaches are principally diﬀerent, they will be clearly distinguished
from each other, while setting the requirement that the connection must have suﬃcient capacity
by both approaches for verifying this. When using the second approach the adhesive thickness
and ﬁllet radius will be assumed equal, whereby the design will also comply with [B2]. Further-
more, as it is unknown at what depth in the adherent failure will occur, the failure depth, tf, will
conservatively be kept to zero, as increased depth reduces the stresses. The actual calculations
used in the analysis will be done by using the MatLab script shown in appendix C for the ﬁrst
approach and appendix D for the second approach.
It shall be noted that both approaches are simpliﬁcations and do not take the full anisotropy
of the materials into account, and both [D5] and [C4] agree that the analytical results shall
only be indicative and further veriﬁed by physical or numerical experiments. This will to some
extent be done as part of this thesis, as a full scale experiment will be performed as part of the
experimental work performed parallel to the theoretical and analytical work of this report.
2.6
Subconclusion
In this chapter, the theoretical background for the further work of this thesis was presented.
This included a presentation of GFRP, with a focus on its mechanical properties and behaviour,
as well as a presentation of approaches for member and connection design of GFRP struc-
tures. Here it was found that the member design could largely be based on Fiberline’s design
guide, which was presented together with some discussions about the methods and the assump-
tions behind these. Capacity control of the bolted connection was likewise based on Fiberline’s
design guide, [D6], while no guidance to the force distribution for rigid connection are given
there. Instead, three diﬀerent approaches were presented, based on distributions used for timber
structures. The diﬀerent approaches consisted of an increasing integration of the eﬀect of the
==PAGE:59==
Chapter 2. Theory
45
anisotropic stiﬀness of the material. Following this, approaches for verifying the bearing capac-
ity of adhesive connections were given, and again diﬀerent approaches were presented. Here the
diﬀerent approaches were based on two diﬀerent sources, being the Eurocomp Design Manual,
[D5] and a Ph.D. thesis studying adhesive joints, [C4]. It was found that the two approaches
used similar methods of determining the peak stresses in the joint, but that the diﬀerences that
exist results in signiﬁcant variations. Furthermore, the two approaches disagree on how the
capacity control shall be done and what failure mechanism will be dominant.
The theory that was presented was focused around the approaches that will be used in the
following analysis chapter. The analysis chapter will thus rely on the theory, approaches and
material properties that were presented in this chapter. For both the bolted and the adhesive
connections, all the diﬀerent approaches presented in this chapter will be used. This is done
in order to identify the variations that the diﬀerent approaches give on the estimated capacity,
relative to each other. The results will thereby be able to identify the potential of making more
precise force distributions in bolted connections, that include the eﬀects from the anisotropic
stiﬀness of the material. For the adhesive connections, the precision of the two diﬀerent ap-
proaches will be investigated through comparison to a Finite Element Model (FEM), to identify
what approach gives the best prediction of the magnitude of the stress peak. The capacity veriﬁ-
cation of the designed adhesive connection, will be done based on both the presented approaches
in order to identify the signiﬁcance that the choice of approach has on the design.
==PAGE:60==
Chapter 3
Structural Analysis
The objective of this chapter, is to carry out a structural analysis of a part of a structure using
GFRP as the sole structural material. Through the analysis, certain challenges are met and
solution methods are suggested.
Likewise, the analysis is done with a selection of diﬀerent
proﬁles and connection types, whereby experience is acquired and guidance given, as to how
structural design is done best using GFRP. The analysis will hereby also include an aspect of
optimization, as diﬀerent solutions will be compared to each other, and thus a superior solution
can be found. While the layout of the examined structure to a large extent already is established,
a design element exists in this report in regards to the connections of the structure. Here general
design considerations are presented, and several designs proposed, which are then evaluated
based on structural and aesthetic evaluations. The examined connections and structural proﬁles
found to be preferable, are used as a basis for the experimental research performed parallel to the
work of this report, controlling whether the physical behaviour corresponds with that expected
based on this analysis.
3.1
The case structure
The house used as a case structure for this analysis is based on a design by Jan Søndergaard,
to which this report seeks to function as an initial step in the complete structural design of the
house. The house is unique, as it is almost purely constructed of GFRP, and has the ambition
of illustrating the architectural potential of buildings with GFRP, as well as act as a prototype
for a possible building system of GFRP. Through the use of a translucent polymer matrix and
46
==PAGE:61==
Chapter 3. Structural Analysis
47
embedded glass ﬁbres, the whole house will be ’alive’, changing with the outdoor environment,
and leaving a contrast in the depth of the shadow of the material, depending on the local density
of the structure. The translucence and visibility of the inherent architecture within the material
is illustrated in ﬁgure 3.1. For further information concerning the internal ﬁbre composition of
the used GFRP proﬁles and how this aﬀects the inherent architecture of the material, reference
is made to appendix E, where some of the work done by Vicki Thake in parallel to the work of
this thesis is shown.
Figure 3.1:
Illustration of GFRP’s translucence property and visible inherent architecture
(from Vicki Thake).
In regards to structural engineering, these ambitions for the building poses some challenges, as a
conventional approach of replacing the critical structural members with steel would compromise
the vision of the house. Constructing the entire structural system with GFRP, likewise gives the
opportunity to illustrate the potential of GFRP as a structural building material, by utilizing
GFRP’s promising mechanical properties. The challenge lies in the lacking norms and traditions
to guide the utilization of these properties. This report aims at contributing to the development
of such traditions.
3.1.1
Building layout
In the following, the layout of the house is presented, to give a understanding of the background
and practical utilization of the analysis performed here. The general concept of the house is
shown in ﬁgure 3.2. It consists of fully closed fa¸cades along the sides, and large window panels
at either end, one of which is lifted, inclining above a terrace area. In the centre of the building
==PAGE:62==
Chapter 3. Structural Analysis
48
is an enclosed garden, with a narrow integrated greenhouse to one of the sides. This, together
with the general room distribution and the overall dimensions, are shown in a plan view on
ﬁgure 3.3. Here, the regularity of the dimensions shall be noted, being a part of the logical
structure of the house. Also the transversely, 2 meter spaced frames shall be noted, which are
also shown on the elevation in ﬁgure 3.4. These frames are the focal point of this structural
analysis and are presented together with the overall structural system in the following parts of
this section. A more detailed description of the frame alone is given in subsection 3.1.3.
