3.1.2
Structural system
In the following, the general structural system is presented.
This subsection highlights the
existing challenges that need to be faced in the structural design of the house, while only the
regular frames are addressed in detail as the scope of this report. The structural system is
presented in ﬁgure 3.5 to 3.9 through a possible construction sequence, in order to illustrate the
diﬀerent elements of the structure. The ﬁrst step is laying out the foundation. This is shown in
ﬁgure 3.5. The foundation is a pole foundation, consisting of crosses of GFRP elements. The
foundation poles are connected to a continuous horizontal plate element, placed on stabilizing
gravel. The detail of the foundation will not be address further in this report, as it is wished to
keep focus on the regular frames and work with these in depth. However, a control of the risk
of crushing and buckling of the pole proﬁles, will be performed, as this is found to be part of
the analyses of the frame. This control is performed in subsection 3.3.4.
Upon the foundation poles, frames are erected.
The frames constitute the main structural
elements in the house, and are a conceptually repeating member in the entire house. A total
of ten similar frames are erected, spanning along the transverse direction of the house’s length.
The end frame, being part of the inclined end of the roof, is slightly higher than the rest, to
meet the inclination of the roof. The remaining nine identical frames are the focus point of this
structural analysis. These are chosen to be the scope, as they are considered to be the basic
==PAGE:64==
Chapter 3. Structural Analysis
50
Figure 3.5: Foundation layout.
structural element, forming the core of the rest of the structure, which are to follow the same
concept as these frame. The nine frames are analysed both in regards to the capacity of the
beam and column members, as well as a detailed investigation of the connection between the
two members, as a bolted and adhesive connection.
In addition to the regular frames, a structure must be designed for the inclined end of the house,
which can support the large glass panel that will be erected here. Likewise, a solution must be
found for the two larger gaps existing between the regular frames in the centre of the building
around the internal garden. Here it is suggested to use transverse spanning beams in the ﬂoor,
to which a small part of the frame member is attached in order to have a column to support the
fa¸cade. These special elements are added in the structural sketch of ﬁgure 3.7. The elements
added here cause some structural challenges that must be addressed, which will, however, not
be done as part of the scope of this report. The transverse beams around the internal gardens,
must be designed to be connected directly to the regular frames. Likewise the connection of the
column here must be made to these transverse beams. The structure at the inclined part of the
house must be able to transfer the forces to the remaining structure, acting as a cantilever. Here
it is suggested to add frames spanning in the direction of the length of the house, activating
two foundation poles, which can transfer the moment from the cantilever through a force couple
to the ground. To distribute the forces eﬀectively to the other frames and the foundation, it
could be advantageous to utilize the potential plate eﬀect of the fa¸cade. The structure shown
==PAGE:65==
Chapter 3. Structural Analysis
51
Figure 3.6: Regular frames in structural system.
on ﬁgure 3.7 concludes the skeleton of the structure, to which the roof, ﬂoors and fa¸cades are
added, which will contribute to the structure as transversely stabilizing elements.
In ﬁgure 3.8, the roof and ﬂoor elements are added, which span along the length of the house,
over the frames. The possibility of long proﬁles from pultrusion, mentioned in subsection 2.1.3,
should be utilized here, to have fewer elements and connections, and allow these proﬁles to
span over several frames, reducing deﬂections. It is noted, that no roof is shown above the
green house area, where a glass structure, with supporting framing, will be build. This is not
considered as part of the structural system, and will not be further addressed. On the other side
of the enclosed garden, there are roof elements spanning four meters, while the other parts of
the roof only spans two meters. This sets additional requirements for the roof elements, which
possibly need strengthening in this area, which is another challenge that should be addressed,
but is not part of the scope of this report.
The ﬁnal element added to the structure is the fa¸cade, which is present along the entire length
of the building, as shown in ﬁgure 3.9. Here the possibility of long GFRP elements, produced
by pultrusion, has great potential for implementation. As the fa¸cade is continuous along the
entire length of the building, it can be utilized as a transversely stabilizing element. Within the
==PAGE:66==
Chapter 3. Structural Analysis
52
Figure 3.7: Special structural elements in structural system.
Figure 3.8: Structural system with added roof and ﬂoor.
==PAGE:67==
Chapter 3. Structural Analysis
53
scope of this report, the dimensioning of the fa¸cade will not be performed, and neither will the
detailing of the connection between the fa¸cades and the frames. The holes remaining in ﬁgure
3.9, are to be window panels. These window panels alone invoke challenges, as they are to be
connected to the frame, and especially the large window panels at the ends of the house, will
create large forces due to the self-weight of the glass, which must be eﬃciently transferred to
the foundations.
Figure 3.9: Structural system with added fa¸cades.
As stated above, there are several challenges involved with the structural design of the house
of GFRP, and far from all these challenges will be addressed in this report. Thus the report
shall not be seen as the structural assessment of house, but rather as a report that seeks to
highlight some aspects of structural design with GFRP elements, using this case as the basis
of this investigation. The investigation is based on the regular frame, reappearing through the
house, and being the primary structural elements, and the basis of the structural concept.
3.1.3
Frame structure
In this subsection the frame is described in more detail, being the main subject of the structural
analysis. An isometric view of the frame is shown in ﬁgure 3.10. It is noticed that the frame
==PAGE:68==
Chapter 3. Structural Analysis
54
is very slender, having an expected thickness of only about 40mm, likely making it dependent
on the roof, ﬂoor and walls to stabilize against lateral torsional buckling, and general buckling.
The other dimensions of the frame are shown in ﬁgure 3.11. The supports are placed away from
the edge, partly to obtain a lighter architectural expression of the building, and partly based on
a structural consideration of limiting the span and deﬂection of the bottom horizontal member.
In this report, the horizontal members are addressed as beam members, and the vertical as
column members. In both ﬁgure 3.10 and 3.11, the frame appears to be a single continuous
element. This is desired, both architecturally and structurally, in the sense, that it is wished
to have a completely rigid frame. A rigid frame is clearly preferred in this house to ensure the
transverse stability of the house, while the stability along the length is ensured by the large
fa¸cades, acting as plates. A rigid frame is also especially advantageous based on the properties
of GFRP. Due to the low modulus of elasticity, the dimensioning factor will tend to be the
deﬂection, which for a ten meter span as present here, would doubtless be the case if the frame
corner did not transfer rotation.
Figure 3.10: Isometric view of frame.
Based on the dimension given here, the frame is analysed in the following, both in regards to
the actual frame members, and the rigid connections in the corners.
==PAGE:69==
Chapter 3. Structural Analysis
55
Figure 3.11: Frame layout.
3.1.4
Materials and proﬁles
The house is to be constructed of GFRP, with characteristics as described in section 2.1. The
design of this case structure is carried out solely by the use of standard proﬁles from Fiberline
Composites. The proﬁles used in the analysis are shown on ﬁgure 3.12, while some properties
of the diﬀerent proﬁles are shown in table 3.1. The data sheets of these proﬁles are found in
appendix F. It is seen that the strip proﬁle is available in diﬀerent thicknesses, of which a 3 mm
and 6 mm is chosen here for the investigation. Studying the data sheets, it is further noticed
that a E-modulus of 23 GPa parallel to the direction of pultrusion is assigned to the plank
proﬁles, while an E-modulus of 17 GPa is assigned the strip proﬁle. Dialogue with Fiberline has
revealed that the diﬀerence of the E-moduli is due to the geometry of the cross-section, rather
than diﬀerences in the material. The strip proﬁle consists of three layers of glass-ﬁbre. The top
and bottom layer being mats, while the middle layer being roving. The roving layer is stronger
and stiﬀer, as described in section 2.1, but as it is in the centre of the cross-section, bending
will happen with small stresses building up in the roving. However, when the strip is used in
combination with the plank and furthermore turned vertically, the rovings will be fully utilized,
making the proﬁle stiﬀer and an E-modulus of 23 GPa can be used parallel to the direction of
pultrusion.
It is noticed, that the moment of inertia of the HD Plank given in table 3.1, is signiﬁcantly
lower than that given in the data sheet in appendix F. The value given in table 3.1 is calculated

3.2
Loads
The loads, that are the basis for the structural analysis, are determined and combined in corre-
spondence with Eurocode 0 ([D1]), Eurocode 1 ([D2]) and the corresponding national annexes
([D3] and [D4]), as well as the speciﬁcations of [D6], mentioned in subsection 2.3.1. The detailed
speciﬁcation of the diﬀerent loads are presented in appendix G, to which reference is made for
the detailed magnitude of the applied loads.
3.2.1
Load cases used in model
The diﬀerent types of loads applied in the analysis are summarized in table 3.2. In the table,
the category is also stated together with the duration in which the load acts. This is due to
the mechanical properties of the material being signiﬁcantly more reduced in case of long term
loading, as described in subsection 2.3.1. The duration of the loading is based on the deﬁnition
of [D6], stating that long term loading is measured in years. This leaves only the dead loads to
be long term loading, as well as a limited part of the live load, reduced by a factor 0.3, as is
suggested for long term live loading of timber structure in [A2].

Strength veriﬁcation of frame
3.3.1
Behaviour of frame structure
Figure 3.13: Numbering of beams and nodes in frame (’#’ indicates node numbering).
Figure 3.14: Displacement of frame for MD Plank+Strip(6mm) for even distributed load from
load case C1a (Dominant snow). Exaggerated by a factor 50.
The displacement of the frame subject to the load case C1a, found to be the most critical load
combination for deﬂections, is shown in ﬁgure 3.14. The displacement shows that the designed
frame works in a beneﬁcial way regarding to the deﬂection, as vertical load on the roof and
dead load of the structure will have a lifting eﬀect on the ﬂoor, counteracting the displacement
that will occur from the live load. This yields that deﬂections due to the live load, will be very
limited, as the dead load of the structure pushes the opposite way. The calculation of these
deﬂections as well as the sectional forces are performed in the program STAADPro.
==PAGE:74==

the SLS loads and the sectional forces based on the ULS loads. As mentioned in subsection
2.3.1, the partial safety coeﬃcient for long term loading is signiﬁcantly higher than that for
short term loading, therefore maximum sectional forces are given both in regards to long term
and short term loading, as it is unknown which will turn out to be most critical. The sectional
forces given in the table are the maximum absolute values and are all given as absolute values.

Studying the variations of the values from the tables and comparing for the diﬀerent proﬁles,
it is seen that the deﬂections change dramatically, while the sectional forces remain more or
less constant. This shows the limited eﬀect the self-weight has on the structure due to the low
density of the material and lightness of the proﬁles. The largest deﬂections occur in the top
beam, as shown in table 3.4. Setting the absolute limit of the deﬂection as l/200, with l = 10
m, being the span length, it is seen that three of the proﬁles exceed the limit of umax = 50.0
mm for the top beam. As mentioned in subsection 2.3.2, the limit of l/200 is the least strict
limit that is recommended in [D6]. The strictest end of the recommended interval is l/400,
which none of the eight investigated proﬁles would fulﬁl. Due to the low E-modulus/strength
ratio of GFRP, setting such a strict limit to the deﬂection would results in a signiﬁcant under
utilization of the bearing capacity of the material, and it is therefore found suitable to operate
with the more tolerable end of the recommended scale.
It is seen that the largest moment occurs above the supports, in node 6 and 7, while the largest
corner moment, where it must be transferred through a connection, occurs in node 3 and 4. In
the top beam member (element 2) there is also a bending moment acting in the middle of the
beam, with approximately the same magnitude as that in node 3 and 4. However, this moment
will not be critical, as it is smaller than that in node 6 and 7 and was therefore not included in
the tables. As an illustration of the section force distribution from the tables above, the curves
for the sectional forces are shown in ﬁgure 3.15 to 3.17, for the load case C5a, which was in
many cases found to be the critical.

In the following two subsections, capacity veriﬁcations will be done for the beam and column
members, respectively. This is done based on the sectional forces given in tables 3.4 to 3.6.
==PAGE:77==
Chapter 3. Structural Analysis
63
3.3.2
Beam members
In this subsection, the capacity of the beam members will be veriﬁed. This is done on the
basis of the maximum stress in the beam, both from the combined action of the moment and
axial forces, as well as from the shear forces. It is assumed that the beams are restrained from
lateral torsional buckling by the ﬂoor and roof structure, together with the internal and external
cladding. Hereby, both the top and bottom of both beam members are supported transversely
against lateral torsional buckling, which would otherwise likely be critical, due to the slenderness
of the beams. In ﬁgure 3.18, the magnitude of stresses in the frame is shown to the left, while
locations for particular need for transverse support for stability is shown to the right, which is
areas of high compression.
Figure 3.18:
Left: Stress distribution in frame for load case C5a.
Right: locations with
particular need for transverse support.
The cross-section properties of the beams, relevant for the capacity veriﬁcation performed here,
are given in table 3.7. Here, both a value for the shear area, used in the procedure presented in
[D6], and the statical moment are given. This shear area is set, simply as the area of the web, as
discussed in subsection 2.3.2. The statical moment is used in Grasshof’s equation to determine
the shear stresses, for which the equation is also given in subsection 2.3.2. Using Grasshof’s
equation is considered more precise and is thus used for the capacity veriﬁcation. The shear
area is included to investigate the variation that may occur from the two diﬀerent approaches
for determining the shear stresses.
The largest sectional forces are found to act in the bottom beam, (element 4-5-6), by the
supports (node 6/7), so the capacity is controlled here. This element is most critical in regards
to axial as well as shear stresses, as is found by studying the values of table 3.4 and 3.6. The
maximum normal stresses occurring in this member, from a combination of moment and axial

In table 3.9, the shear stresses and the utilization of the shear capacity is shown for all the
proﬁles, for both long and short term loading. The shear stresses are given, both based on the
shear area and the statical moment, presented in table 3.7. It is seen that the shear stresses by
using Grasshof’s equation, based on the statical moment, are generally 50 % higher, than when
using the shear area, which stems from the parabola shape of the Grasshof shear distribution.
==PAGE:79==
Chapter 3. Structural Analysis
65
This indicates that ﬁnding the shear stresses simply by dividing with the area of the web, is an
unsafe approximation of the actual shear stresses. The degree of utilization presented in table
3.9, are based on the shear stresses found by Grasshof’s equation. As for the normal stresses in
table 3.8, it is seen that the utilization of the shear capacity is consequently higher when based
on the long term loading. It is also seen, that the shear stresses prove to be much more critical
than the normal stresses, with two of the proﬁles having their capacity exceeded, being the MD
and HD plank alone. As the shear is predominantly carried by the webs, it is seen that the
diﬀerence in utilization between the proﬁles with the MD plank and HD plank, is insigniﬁcant.

Based on the capacity veriﬁcation of the beams performed in this subsection, it is indicated
that shear capacity is the most critical aspect for beams, when stability and deﬂection limits
are disregarded. It is also seen that the long term loads, though signiﬁcantly lower, can be
the most critical, due to the greatly reduced capacity in long term loading. As mentioned in
subsection 2.3.2, requirements for the combination of the shear and normal stress are not given
in [D6]. In this case, it is estimated to be reasonable not to combine the stresses, as the stress
distribution will be as shown in ﬁgure 3.19 for the MD+Strip (6 mm) proﬁle. It is seen that
the largest shear stress is present when the normal stress is almost zero and vice versa. As the
ribs are small, there is not a location where both shear and normal stress are high, such as the
meeting between the ﬂange and web of an I-proﬁle.
==PAGE:80==
Chapter 3. Structural Analysis

Figure 3.19: Shear and normal stress distribution in MD + Strip (6 mm) proﬁle.
3.3.3
Column members
In the following, the capacity of the column members of the frame are investigated. This will
be done based on combined moment and axial actions, taking buckling into account.
The
veriﬁcation is done both for the strong and weak axes of the column members, to determine
whether the proﬁles have suﬃcient capacity and whether transverse stabilizing is neccessary. In
table 3.10, the properties relevant for this veriﬁcation are shown, in addition to those already
presented in table 3.7. The properties given are the moment of inertia around the weak axis
of the proﬁles, as well as the critical column load. The critical column load is given for both
the strong and weak axis, and for short and long term loading, as this value, like the strength
properties, is strongly reduced under long term loading. Due to the broad but thin proﬁles, the
critical column load is very low around the weak axis, but very large around the strong axis.
Apart from a signiﬁcantly higher moment of inertia around this axis, it is also due to the column
being assumed ﬁxed in both ends around this axis, due to the rigid connections of the frame.
This results in a halving of the critical column length. However, for the weak axis, the column
does not have the same rigidity at the ends, and is therefore considered as a simply supported
column, giving the full length as a critical column length.
Already by comparing the critical column loads in table 3.10 with the compressive load in the
column, shown in table 3.5, it is seen that most of the proﬁles will buckle around the weak axis,
both due to long and short term loading. In table 3.11, the stresses in the column members
are shown, calculated based on the method given in subsection 2.3.2, taking the axial force,
moment and buckling into account. It is seen that most of the proﬁle has ’∞’ written as both
the stress and utilization. This means that the critical column load is exceeded and the column

The investigation in this subsection, has shown that the capacity of the columns are generally
over utilized for the weak axis of the proﬁles, while poorly utilized around the strong axis. This
is unsurprising as the columns are both much stiﬀer around the strong axis and have a shorter
critical column length. Obviously, more quadratic columns cross-sections would be preferred to
obtain more equal utilization in both directions. However, in this speciﬁc case study, the layout
of the building allows easy implementation of transverse support through the fa¸cade. This will
be neccessary for all proﬁles, except the proﬁle consisting of 2 HD proﬁles.
3.3.4
Supporting poles
Here the capacity of the poles, supporting the frame, is veriﬁed in regards to local crushing of
the frame on the contact with the pole support. The foundation principle is illustrated in ﬁgure
3.20. As part of the concept of the house as an architectural feature, it is desired to construct
the foundation of GFRP, as the rest of the house. The sections are proposed to be identical
to the proﬁles of the frame. The height of the poles are 1500 mm, whereof 500 mm are above
ground. Two proﬁles are merged together to form a cross, which will prevent buckling around
the weak axis. The cross is then attached to a T-proﬁle, which lies on a layer of stabilizing
gravel.
==PAGE:83==
Chapter 3. Structural Analysis
69
Figure 3.20: Isometric view of foundation concept.
To ensure the bearing capacity, it should be checked whether the proﬁles of the frame will crush
due to the contact pressure, creating compression perpendicular to the ﬁbres of the web. As
a conservative estimate, it is assumed that the entire reaction force is distributed over an area
equal to the total thickness of the webs of the frame proﬁle, multiplied by the length of the
support of 500 mm. This is conservative, as the ﬁbres in the neighbouring part of the proﬁle
will also be compressed, and a longer support length could thus be used, as is done for timber
structure, [A10]. The capacity against crushing is veriﬁed by:
σc,90,d =

(3.1)
With Rmax being the largest reaction force, tweb the total web thickness, lsupport the length of
the support and Asupport the area that the reaction force is distributed over. In table 3.13, the
utilization of the capacity for compression perpendicular to the direction of pultrusion is shown.
The utilization is given for all the proﬁles and for both long and short term loading.
It is seen that the risk of crushing of the proﬁles with contact to the supports is not critical for
any of the proﬁles, but it is noticed that the long term loading is more critical than the short

3.3.5
Frame member evaluation
In this section, the eight diﬀerent considered proﬁles have been veriﬁed in regards to their bearing
capacity. Based on an evaluation of the results of this performed investigation, a favoured proﬁle
will be chosen to be used for the frame of the case study. In table 3.14 a summary is given of the
diﬀerent proﬁles, including the mass, the largest utilization of the capacity and the utilization
of the acceptable deﬂection interval. The critical stress was found to be the shear stress of
the bottom beam under long term loading, while the critical deﬂection was that of the top
beam. This is under the assumption that all the frame members are restrained from buckling
and lateral torsional buckling out of the plane of the frame. Based on these values, the most
suitable proﬁle is the one with the combined lowest utilization and lowest mass.
It can be observed that three of the considered proﬁles, exceed the available capacity in regards
to deﬂection, and two in regards the bearing capacity. The proﬁle with the lowest mass that is
still within the limits, is a HD Plank with a 3 mm strip added, making it, based on the values in
table 3.14 alone, the most suitable proﬁle. However, in regards to connection design, the proﬁle
satisfying the requirement, with the second lowest mass, being the MD Plank with an added 6

mm strip, could be regarded as superior. The mass is 6% higher, and the deﬂection utilization
5%-points lower, while the bearing capacity is as much as 25%-points lower. This is due to the
increased web thickness of the ’MD Plank + Strip (6 mm)’, reducing the shear stresses, while
not contributing much to the deﬂection reduction. However, this increased web thickness is
also advantageous in connection design, as the bolt capacity is directly proportional to the web
thickness, as stated in section 2.4. Likewise for an adhesive connection, stresses will be more
evenly distributed when the webs are stiﬀer, as mentioned in section 2.5. The reduced amount
of ribs in the MD Plank also eases the possibility of an internal gusset plate in the connection.
The other proﬁles that fulﬁlled the requirements have a increased mass of at least additionally
9%, and likewise have the disadvantage of an increased amount of ribs, complicating elegant
connections, and therefore found less ideal.
Based on these consideration, and the prediction of more ﬂexible connection design options, it
is chosen to use the MD Plank with an added 6 mm strip for the frame. This proﬁle fulﬁls the
requirements, both in regards to the load bearing capacity and the deﬂection. Additionally it
allows a large safety margin, as only 60% of the load bearing capacity is used, and the deﬂection
limit will be reached signiﬁcantly before the bearing capacity.
3.4
Bolted Connection Design
In the previous section, the frame was analysed for its structural performance for eight diﬀerent
proﬁles. Based on the analysis, it was decided to construct the frame of the proﬁle named ’MD
Plank+Strip (6 mm)’. In this section, proposals for a bolted connection for the frame will be
==PAGE:86==
Chapter 3. Structural Analysis
72
presented, based on this proﬁle. The section begins with a summary of some of the initial design
considerations that were the basis for the following four diﬀerent bolted connection suggestions.
These four diﬀerent connection designs are presented together with the calculations performed
in relation to each of them. The section will end with a discussion of the four diﬀerent options
and a overview of some of the experiences gathered in the analysis. This part of the analysis is
therefore principally diﬀerent than that performed in the previous section. The previous section
was based on calculations of existing proﬁles, while this section has a strong element of design,
were diﬀerent options have been proposed, considered and evaluated based on their suitability
to the situation and requirement set by the frame that was analysed in the previous section.
Figure 3.21: Moment curve for load case C5a.
Table 3.15:
Load on connection from analysis of frame for MD Plank + Strip (6mm).
M

[kNm]
[kNm]
[kN]

19.5
The connections are designed based on the hardest loaded corner of the frame, found through
the structural analysis of the frame in section 3.3. The main challenge of the connection is to
transfer the moment, as this will give a distribution that will load some bolts signiﬁcantly more
than others, dependent on the layout of the bolts. In ﬁgure 3.21, the moment distribution for
the C5a load case is shown, which is the load case found to give the largest moments in the
corners. Here it appears, that the hardest loaded corners, in regards to the moment force, are
the connections of the top beam. Therefore the top beam with the load from load case C5a
will be the basis of the structural calculations of the connections, giving the forces shown in
==PAGE:87==
Chapter 3. Structural Analysis
73
table 3.15. It is noticed that these forces are for short term loading, while it was found for the
frame members in section 3.3 that the long term loading tended to be most critical and that
the shear load was most critical load. However, during the investigation of the connections, it
was found that the shear load is not critical to the connection design, as this can be assumed
evenly distributed over all bolts. As for basing the connection design on the short term load
rather than the long term load, it was found that the largest short term load was a factor of 2.60
larger than that for long term loading, while the capacity is a factor of 2.46 larger for short term
loading. This means that the load is more increased in short term loading than the capacity in
the connection. This shows that short term loading will be most critical.
In the following analysis of the connection, only a few formulas will be presented, as all the
calculations are based on methods, equations, and mechanical properties already presented in
section 2.4.
3.4.1
Design Considerations
For the design of a bolted connection for the frame structure, two main design choices need to
be made. These are related to the cut made in the meeting of the horizontal and the vertical
proﬁle and to the use of secondary elements such as gusset plates and angle proﬁles.
In regards to the cuts, four diﬀerent concepts are considered, illustrated in ﬁgure 3.22. The top
two concepts are simple meetings of the two elements without needing any cutting. Here the
left version has the advantage of being able to transfer vertical forces through contact. Both
these concepts have a disadvantage of leaving open sections at either the side or the top, which
could be found to be aesthetically displeasing. The bottom left concept, with a diagonal cut,
has a single cut of both proﬁles, and leaves the frame completely free from openings, giving
potential for an aesthetically pleasing connection, as, if performed well, it could give the feeling
of the frame being a single continuous element. The bottom right concept is a step concept,
following the ribs inside the proﬁle. This has an advantage of a large amount of horizontal and
vertical contact between the proﬁles, but leaves a small opening and is complex to cut.
The gusset plate or angle proﬁle can either be placed externally or internally within the proﬁle.
An external connection could either be a gusset plate on the sides of the frame, as sketches
in ﬁgure 3.23, left, or angle proﬁles on the inner and outer side of the frame, as sketches in
ﬁgure 3.23, right. These solutions are simple and independent of the internal geometry of the
==PAGE:88==
Chapter 3. Structural Analysis
74
Figure 3.22: Cut concepts for the joint.
proﬁles. However they are visually very obvious and risk compromising the architectural vision.
Additionally, they will require some inﬁll of the hollow proﬁles, to allow tightening of the bolts,
without damaging the proﬁles. Alternatively, the gusset plates or angles can be placed within
the proﬁles. This means that either the internal ribs must dictate the geometry and location
of the angles, so they are placed between the internal ribs, as sketches in ﬁgure 3.24 left, or
the internal ribs must be removed locally around the connection, as will be neccessary in the
sketches shown in ﬁgure 3.24 right. The decision of cut and gusset plate must harmonise, as
some cut options are more appropriate for diﬀerent gusset solutions and vice versa.
In the following, four diﬀerent connection proposals are presented. One without a cut, as shown
top left in ﬁgure 3.22 and an external gusset plate. The second has a diagonal cut, as shown
bottom left in ﬁgure 3.22 and with internal gusset plate, as shown right in ﬁgure 3.24. The third
likewise has a diagonal cut, as shown bottom left in ﬁgure 3.22, but with internal angles, as
shown left in ﬁgure 3.24. The fourth and last connection is a design illustrating an alternative
to the two dimensional thinking of the cuts of ﬁgure 3.22 and instead trying to merge the
proﬁles into each other, inspired by methods of traditional carpenter joints. In the calculations
of the connections, it is generally assumed that the gusset plate will have suﬃcient bearing
==PAGE:89==
Chapter 3. Structural Analysis
75
Figure 3.23: External gusset plate solutions concepts.
Figure 3.24: Internal gusset plate solutions concepts.
capacity and consist of a isotropic GFRP plate, and that the failure will thus occur in the frame
members. Such an isotropic plate could be created by gluing plates with opposite direction of
pultrusion together. All the connections will be performed using M18 bolts, to allow a more
direct comparison between the diﬀerent connections. Following the presentation of the four
diﬀerent connections, a comparison can thus be performed, based on the parameters: Number
of bolts, utilization of capacity, and area of gusset plate used. These are the quantitatively easily
measurable parameters, which the connections will be compared on the basis of. However, other
at least as important factors, will also be taken into account in the comparison. This involves
ease of assembly and fabrication, as well as the aesthetic quality of the connection. This last
parameter is very important in this speciﬁc case study, as one of the main purposes of the case
structure is to display architectural qualities of the material.
==PAGE:90==
Chapter 3. Structural Analysis
76
3.4.2
Bolted Connection type 1: Gusset plate connection, without proﬁle
cut
The ﬁrst of the four investigated bolted connections consist of the beam lying directly on the
column, while the two are connected by an external gusset plate reaching the entire height of the
beam and width of the column. A concept sketch of the connection is shown in ﬁgure 3.25, while
a layout of the connection is shown on ﬁgure 3.26. The distribution of the bolts are arranged
so it meets the requirement of minimum distances.
Figure 3.25: Concept of connection type 1.
Figure 3.26: Layout of connection 1 (d=18 mm).
Using the approach presented in section 2.4, the bolt force in the connection is calculated for
three diﬀerent scenarios. The ﬁrst scenarios disregards the E-modulus and sets the direction
of the bolt force from the moment, as perpendicular to a straight line from the centre of the
connection, as would be done for a isotropic material. This distribution is shown on ﬁgure 3.27.
Here the circle illustrates the hardest loaded bolt in the connection, in relation to the capacity of
==PAGE:91==

the bolt. The second scenario distributes the forces dependent on the fraction of the E-moduli,
as described in more detail in section 2.4. This distribution is shown in ﬁgure 3.28. The last
investigated scenario, assumes that the entire bolt force from the moment follows the direction
of pultrusion, having the highest E-modulus, while the shear obviously gives bolt forces in the
shear direction. This distribution is shown in ﬁgure 3.29.

capacity of the structure to take the eﬀect of the modulus of elasticity into account. It is clear
that the case where the eﬀect of the E-moduli is disregarded, gives the most critical distribution,
where the capacity is utilized by 90%. Here the utilization is dominated by the force component

perpendicular to the direction of pultrusion. In this direction the capacity is about double as
utilized as the capacity parallel to the direction of pultrusion. Whether using the distribution
of proportionality with the E-moduli, or letting the component from the moment be parallel
to the pultrusion direction gives almost the same results. This observation is of great interest,
as it indicates that using a simple distribution of all force components parallel to the largest
E-modulus gives precise results, assuming that the distribution with proportionality with the
E-moduli is closest to the distribution that will occur in reality. If this is generally the case, it
would be allowable to greatly simplify hand calculations of the bearing capacity of rigid joint
of GFRP, as it is much easier to base the calculation solely on the distance from the bolt to the
centre perpendicular to the pultrusion direction, than the other distributions considered. It will
therefore be interesting to see whether the same is the case for the following connections. When
the force distribution is proportional with the E-moduli, the utilization in both directions are
approximately the same. This is desirable, as it potentially allows maximum utilization of the
capacity of the material. When the force distribution is solely in the direction of pultrusion,
the utilization is naturally predominantly in the direction of pultrusion, where the capacity is
so much larger, that the entire force component can be supported without reaching too large
utilizations.
The connection investigated here is simple and logical, but it is not necessarily architecturally
pleasing, as the gusset plate is very obvious and very large. Furthermore, it will be neccessary
to ﬁll out the hollow parts of the proﬁle, where the bolts are placed to avoid crushing of
the thin proﬁle webs when tightening the bolts.
The ﬁlling and the large gusset plate will
together result in a rather heavy connection. Here, it has been assumed that all loads must

3.4.3
Bolted Connection type 2: Internal connection structure with diagonal
cut
The second investigated connection has a diagonal cut and a internal gusset plate. The design
concept is shown on ﬁgure 3.30, where the outer plate is not shown, to make the internal gusset
plate visible. The layout of the connection is shown on ﬁgure 3.31. The connection consist of a
total of 40 M18 bolts, which was found neccessary in order to obtain suﬃcient capacity for all
the force distribution scenarios.
Figure 3.30: Concept of connection type 2.
==PAGE:94==
Chapter 3. Structural Analysis
80
Figure 3.31: Layout of connection 2 (d=18mm).
The plot in ﬁgure 3.32 to 3.34 shows a similar variation dependent on the E-modulus as was
observed in subsection 3.4.2. The plots reveals very small utilization of the bolts in the middle,
especially when assuming distribution solely parallel to the direction of pultrusion, where these
bolts do not contribution to the distribution of the moment at all. Though small, the contri-
bution from these bolts was found to be neccessary in order to avoid exceeding the capacity in
the beam member when disregarding the diﬀerences in E-moduli. Alternatively, the bolts could
be spread more out, increasing the distance to the centre, but this would require an increased
gusset plate. Due to the restriction of four bolts per row, mentioned in section 2.4, it was not
possible to add more bolts except these in the middle. It could be argued that the connection
has ﬁve bolts in diagonal rows. However, as these are neither following a ﬁbre or force direction,
it is not believed to be critical.
Examining the utilization of the hardest loaded bolt in table 3.17, it is found to be very similar
to that observed from table 3.16. However, the results from this connection show an even larger
decrease in the utilization when assuming a force distribution from the moment dependent on
the E-modulus.
This is due to the bolt layout in subsection 3.4.2 being double symmetric
around the centre, resulting in the four most critical bolts being equally hard loaded by the
moment when disregarding the eﬀect of the E-modulus. When the loads are fully distributed
in the direction of pultrusion, there are eight bolts being most critical and equally loaded.
Meanwhile, the bolt layout here, leaves only two bolts to be hardest loaded from the moment
when disregarding the eﬀect of the E-moduli, while eight bolts are equally hard loaded when the
distribution is solely in the direction of pultrusion. For this reason, a larger beneﬁt is achieved

3.4.4
Bolted Connection type 3: Internal angled and diagonal cut
This connection is likewise a diagonal cut, but rather than an internal gusset plate, it has
internal angle proﬁles, ﬁtted between the internal ribs of the proﬁles as shown on the concept
sketch in ﬁgure 3.35. The angle proﬁles have a thickness matching the hollow section of the
proﬁles, being 36 mm, and has a width of 95 mm, and are 410 mm long, as shown on the layout
on ﬁgure 3.36.
Figure 3.35: Concept of connection type 3.
As the angles are separate proﬁles and not a continuous plate where the forces can be distributed,
the moment cannot be directly based on the distance to the centroid of the connection as for
the previous two connections. Likewise a distribution of forces from the moment following the
==PAGE:97==
Chapter 3. Structural Analysis
83
Figure 3.36: Layout of connection 3.
direction of pultrusion is not possible, as this would require stabilizing forces in the cut, to
obtain equilibrium, as sketched in ﬁgure 3.37.
Figure 3.37: Equilibrium sketch for connection 3.
Instead it is neccessary to make a statically admissible force distribution to each of the angles,
ensuring equilibrium within the angle proﬁle. The forces that must be distributed are assumed
to act in the centre of the height of the frame members, at the point of the diagonal cut, as
illustrated in ﬁgure 3.38.
While the shear forces are simply distributed evenly to all bolts, several admissible distributions
of the moment were investigated. The admissible distribution giving the lowest load, meaning
the one closest to the actual distribution, was found by assuming all bolt forces to have a 45
degree angle. This ensures equilibrium in the angles, as the same forces will act in the same
direction in both halves of the angle. Projecting the bolt location perpendicularly onto the 45
==PAGE:98==
Chapter 3. Structural Analysis
84
Figure 3.38: Forces on connection of connection 3
degree inclined cut axis of the connection, the distance of the bolt from the centre is measured
along this line and the forces are assigned by:

there are equilibrium in all the angle proﬁles, as the force distribution is completely mirrored
around the cut line.

3.4.5
Bolted Connection type 4: Carpenter inspired connection
The fourth and last of the investigated connections, is inspired by traditional carpenter joints,
as shown in ﬁgure 3.41. The idea was to attempt to merge the two frame members together
rather than being two separate members and an added gusset plate binding them together.
Figure 3.41: Carpenter joint as inspiration of connection (from www.craftsmanspace.com).
The concept of the design connection is illustrated by the sketches in ﬁgure 3.42 to 3.44. The
joint is thought to be able to function both as a bolted or a glued joint, or a combination. The
connection is constructed by both the outer plates being cut away from the horizontal element,
while all the inner stiﬀeners remain. Between the stiﬀeners, additional strengthening elements
are added, either by gluing or bolting. This is shown in ﬁgure 3.42.
Figure 3.42: Beam of the carpenter inspired joint, with and without strengthening elements.
In the vertical elements all the inner ribs are cut away, leaving the two outer plates and the
back of the proﬁle. Hereby the conﬁgured beam element ﬁts within the column and they can
be joined by glue or bolts. This is illustrated in ﬁgure 3.43 and 3.44. As seen, this connection
leaves no openings on the sides and results in a closed and smooth connection.
An ideal solution would be to glue the additional element placed between the ribs of the beam
in position, making them act as a integrated part of the beam. However, here the entire solution
will be investigated as a bolted connection, to be able to compare it with the other connection
==PAGE:102==
Chapter 3. Structural Analysis
88
Figure 3.43: Carpenter inspired joint before assembly.
Figure 3.44: Assembled carpenter inspired joint.
presented previously.
Here it will be assumed that the strengthening element can interact,
due to tight placement, so that they can work as a continuous plate. The layout of the bolted
connection is as shown in ﬁgure 3.45. The bolts are attempted placed from the centre with equal
distance, resulting in similar forces in all bolts, when disregarding the eﬀect of the modulus of
elasticity. The bolts are thus placed to accommodate the bolt distribution, when disregarding
the eﬀect of the moduli of elasticity, challenging the other distributions as being favourable.
In this connection, the placing of the bolts is restrained by the placing of the strengthening
element, which the bolts must go through the centre of.
The force distribution is shown on ﬁgure 3.46 to 3.48 depending on the assumed eﬀect of the E-
moduli on the force distribution. It is seen that this force layout gives an even force distribution
when disregarding the E-moduli, where all bolts are similarly loaded. This distribution would
therefore clearly be preferred over the previously presented ones in case of a isotropic material.
==PAGE:103==
Chapter 3. Structural Analysis
89
Figure 3.45: Bolts in carpenter inspired joint.
However, the circular pattern also results in some bolt forces being almost purely perpendicular
to the direction of pultrusion, where the material is weak. In contrast to the other investigated
connections, it is here found to be most critical to let the force components from the moments
be purely oriented in the direction of pultrusion. This is due to the bolt distribution being
designed for a disregarding of the moduli of elasticity. Here it is found that in the column, two
bolts stand out as being hardest loaded. These two bolts carry such a large share of the load,
that even though it is directed to the strong direction of the material, it becomes close to the
capacity, as can be seen by examining the values of table 3.19. As for the other connections, it is
found that the most beneﬁcial distribution is to assume the distribution to be dependent on the
ratio of the E-moduli. While the other investigated connection showed pure distribution in the
pultrusion direction was only slightly conservative, compared to it being dependent to the ratio
of the E-moduli, it is here found to give almost double as much capacity to distribute based on
the ratio. This is only the case for the column. The beam obtains the lowest utilization when
all forces are directed in the direction of pultrusion.

3.4.6
Comparison of investigated connections
In the previous four subsections, four diﬀerent connection designs have been presented. The
connections were principally diﬀerent, both in regards to cut of proﬁles, gusset plate used,
and number and distribution of bolts.
As a quantitative comparison of these four diﬀerent
connections, table 3.20 gives some comparable parameters for the four diﬀerent connections.
These parameters are; amount of bolts, the area of gusset plates used, and the largest utilization
of the bolt capacity.
The area of the gusset plate is found as an adequate measure of the
material needed for the gusset plates, as the critical factor for the thickness is likely to be due
to the direct contact with the bolts, and therefore similar for all four connections, if they were
equally utilized. This is under the assumption that the gusset plate does not deform and has
suﬃcient capacity. The maximum utilization is shown, as it was found when the bolt forces
were distributed proportionally with the E-moduli, being the distribution believed to be closest
to that which will occur in reality. This was always found to be the most favourable basis of the
force distribution from the moment. This shows an example of how a more advanced calculation
and force distribution pays of by resulting in a more accurate and thus less conservative design,
potentially saving material. In Connection 3, the bolt forces was only distributed one way, as
changing the distribution to be dependent on the E-moduli, would not meet the requirements
of equilibrium in all the angles of the connection. In this connection it was found that the
capacity of the internal angles was more utilized than that from the bolt force. Nonetheless,
the utilization based on the bolted connection is given in table 3.20, so that the utilization of
the same situation is compared.
Table 3.20:
Comparison between the 4 bolted connections.
Amount of bolts
Area of gusset plate
Maximum utilization
[m2]
(proportionality with E-moduli)
Connection 1
32
0.50
55 %
Connection 2
40
0.39
42 %
Connection 3
40
0.34
72 %
Connection 4
28
0.48
52 %
It is seen that based on the values from table 3.20, there is not one connection that stands out
as superior to the others in all aspects. Connection 4 has a signiﬁcantly lower amount of bolts
than the others, which was achieved by a circular distribution of the bolts, activating all the
bolts to a more similar extent. Such a bolt pattern could be used in Connection 1 and 2, while
keeping the cut and gusset plate as they are. Connection 3 on the other hand, is somewhat
==PAGE:106==
Chapter 3. Structural Analysis
92
locked in regards to the placing of the bolts, due to a diﬀerent distribution, as it consist of
several individual proﬁles rather than a continuous gusset plate. The results of this is that it
has signiﬁcantly smaller amount of material used for gusset plate, making the connection lighter.
However, the shape of these angle proﬁles are more complicated to construct. This connection
is also the one where the capacity is most utilized, due to the distribution not being able to be
based on the moduli of elasticity.
Apart from this quantitative comparison, the connections must also be evaluated based on their
aesthetic values, in respect to the architecture they are to be a part of. As the entire house
will be constructed of translucent materials and due to the conceptional thinking behind the
architecture, it is important that even a connection existing within the walls has a visual quality,
as they could still be sensed, based on the impact they have on the light going through. Based
on this, the area of gusset plate becomes even more important, as this will aﬀect the density by
which the connection is conceived. Here connection 3 is preferred, while connection 1 is clearly
unsuitable, as it visually will appear very heavy.
Through dialogue with the other parties involved in the project, it is decided to proceed with
connection 3. This is believed to be the most suitable, as it is relatively light, and can give
a smooth ﬁnish, where the frame is conceived as a continuous element. This is supported by
the cross-section of the proﬁles used, which will remain unchanged, apart from the angles stuck
between the ribs, unlike connection 2 and 4, where the cross-section must be changed locally by
removing the ribs. Additionally, this connection design is suitable for an adhesive connection,
as it has a large degree of contact between the angle proﬁle and the frame members. In the
following section, an adhesive connection with the same design as connection 3 will be performed.
Proceeding with an identically designed bolted and adhesive connection gives the advantage of
a direct comparison between the two connection method. It also gives the advantage that in
practical usage of the design, it will be possible to make a hybrid solution, where the angles are
glued to the beam member in a factory and then bolted to the column on site, to ease assembly
and transport.
As part of the experimental research done parallel to the work of this report, a full scale
experiment performed on a connection similar to connection 3 is performed. Hereby it will be
seen what part of the connection in reality is the most critical, causing the failure. It will also be
seen, whether the connection remains intact during loading, or whether the proﬁles slide apart
already at small loads, due to local deformation. Likewise it will be interesting to see, whether
==PAGE:107==
Chapter 3. Structural Analysis
93
the connection truly is rigid and limits the deformations of the beam as expected. For further
detail and results from this experimental work, reference is made to the experimental report.
Making the experiment on this connection type, unfortunately will not further illustrate the
eﬀect of the diﬀerences in E-moduli on the distribution of the forces. A more detailed study in
this would be very interesting, and allow more detailed design and optimization of rigid bolted
connection of GFRP, which this investigation has shown hold potential for great optimizations
of connections.
3.5
Adhesive Connections Design
In this section, an adhesive connection will be designed and the behaviour and capacity investi-
gated. Unlike the previous section about bolted connection, only one design will be investigated.
This design in similar to the bolted connection 3, with a diagonal cut of the proﬁles and angles
ﬁtted between the ribs. The same loads act on the connection, as in the bolted connection, as
given in table 3.15. The investigation of this adhesive connection will initiate with a general
veriﬁcation of the calculation method for determining the stresses, presented in section 2.5 and
an evaluation of the two approaches. This is found neccessary, as very little reference work,
examples, practical use and veriﬁcation was found on these calculation methods. Due to this,
it was found hard to estimate the precision and reliability of the procedure, as well as con-
trol whether the interpretation and implementation of the procedure was correct or whether
mistakes were made. To feel more conﬁdent in regard to these consideration, a ﬁnite element
model (FEM) in Abaqus was made to support the use of the approaches presented in section
2.5 and highlight the diﬀerences between the two approaches. This veriﬁcation is presented in
subsection 3.5.1.
The choice of adhesive used in the calculations and experiment research performed parallel to
the work of this report, was based on available experience and reference work. The choice was to
use the adhesive Sikadur330, as it is the adhesive predominantly used by Fiberline and used in
the experiments of [C4] together with Fiberline proﬁles. This combined gave a reference dataset
in the design, as well as available experience in the practical usage of the adhesive.
The main data of the adhesive is shown in table 3.21, while the full data sheet from the producer
is shown in Appendix H.
==PAGE:108==
Chapter 3. Structural Analysis
94

While the tensile strength and modulus of elasticity in table 3.21 are from the datasheet, the

It is not ideal that the mechanical properties stems from three
diﬀerent sources, but as none of the sources could provide all the required data, it was neccessary.
The consequence of this, is that the results should be treated with care and evaluated critically.
3.5.1
Method Veriﬁcation by Finite Element Model
In order to verify the two analytical approaches of calculating the stresses in a double lap joint,
as presented in section 2.5, a FEM of a double lap joint is modelled using Abaqus as shown on
ﬁgure 3.49. The veriﬁcation of the method is found to be neccessary, as little practical reference
work is found of the calculation method, even though a large extent of theoretical sources are
available on the topic.
The FEM is modelled as half the double lap joint, with symmetrical boundary conditions along
the bottom, being the middle of the inner part, as shown on ﬁgure 3.49. The outer part is
also supported against transverse movement along the side, to avoid undesirable deformation,
resulting in misleading stresses. The GFRP elements and adhesive layer was constrained to
each other by a ’tie’ constraint between the parts.
Figure 3.49: Setup of Finite Element Model.
==PAGE:109==
Chapter 3. Structural Analysis
95
The model consist of 5 mm thick GFRP elements, deﬁned with appropriate mechanical proper-
ties from table 2.2, as a lamina. The adhesive layer is modelled with a thickness of respectively
1 mm and 2 mm, with mechanical properties likewise deﬁned as a lamina with the values from
table 3.21. Two diﬀerent thicknesses are used, to study the signiﬁcance of the thickness, which
according to the analytical formulations plays a signiﬁcant role. The model is assigned a width
of 1 mm and a load corresponding to F=200 N.
The elements used are of the type: CPS4, being a 4-node bilinear plane stress quadrilateral.
The layout of this element is shown on ﬁgure 3.50.
Figure 3.50: Layout of ﬁnite element, CPS4.
The theoretical background of the analytical formulations, are based upon the shear stress τxy
within the adhesive layer, while the out-of-plane stress and the corresponding shear and stresses
in the adherent, are directly based upon this value of τxy, as described in section 2.5. It is
therefore decided to concentrate this investigation on the stresses in the centre of the adhesive
layer, along the path shown in ﬁgure 3.51. Here both the shear stresses and the out-of-plane
stresses are investigated and compared to the analytical results. Comparison of the maximum
values are done with results from both the approaches from section 2.5, while the distribution
is only compared to that from the second approach, based on [C4], as the ﬁrst approach does
not give expressions for the distribution but only the maximum stresses.
Figure 3.51: Path on Finite Element Model.
To determine an appropriate ﬁneness of the mesh, a convergence analysis was performed, where
the mesh ﬁneness in focus, was the one along the edge of the adhesive layer and within this.
==PAGE:110==
Chapter 3. Structural Analysis
96
The elements in the adhesive layer were kept quadratic, and the elements tied to these in the
GFRP plate were constrained to have the same size. The ﬁneness of the mesh was therefore
measured by the amount of elements along the borderline between the GFRP elements and the
adhesive layer. The convergence analysis was done on a model with a lap length of 100 mm and
a thickness of the adhesive layer of 2 mm. The results of the convergence analysis is shown in
ﬁgure 3.52 for the maximum stresses occurring in the adhesive layer, where the results for the
diﬀerent mesh ﬁnesses are shown together with the analytical result.

Figure 3.52: Convergence plot for shear stress, τxy and out-of-plane stress σz.
It is seen, that both the results for the shear stress, τxy, and out-of-plane stress, σz, converges
to a value somewhat below the analytical result. The two ﬁrst results, for 50 and 100 nodes
along the lap length, are in both cases signiﬁcantly away from the converged result. With 200
nodes, the plot seems to have converged for τxy. The plot for σz is very close to be converged
after 400 nodes, but still a visible drop is seen from 400 to 600 nodes, though of a insigniﬁcant
magnitude. While this plot shows a convergence of the maximum value, it is also of interest to
see how the shape of the stress curve along the adhesive layer is aﬀected by a reﬁnement of the
mesh. The stress curve for the shear stress, τxy, is therefore shown in ﬁgure 3.53, together with
the stress curve according to the analytical approach.
The plot in ﬁgure 3.53 shows that all the diﬀerent investigated ﬁnenesses of the mesh gives
results close to the analytical curve. However, around the edge of the adhesive layer the results
do not correspond completely. This is better seen on ﬁgure 3.54, where a zoom of the plot by
the edge of the adhesive layer is shown. Here it is clear that the two coarser meshes, with 50 and
100 elements along the length of the adhesive layer, do not manage to capture the peak of the
stress curve. This explains why the maximum stress in these cases are somewhat diﬀerent. It is

Figure 3.55: Mesh of Finite Element Model.
In ﬁgure 3.56 and 3.57, plots of the analytical and numerical results for the variation of the shear
force τxy are shown. It is seen, that the numerical results consequently follow the curve of the
analytical results with relatively good precision for both increasing lap lengths and thicknesses.
It is also seen that the peak stresses from the numerical results is consequently lower than that
of the analytical, with a single exception for a lap length of 25 mm and an adhesive thickness
of 1 mm. As expected based on the theory of section 2.5, after a certain lap length, further
increase of the length, simply results in a longer length with zero shear stress, while the peaks
remains more or less unchanged. This is seen when comparing the results with a lap length
of 200 mm and 400 mm. Studying the plots, it is also noticed that some follow the symmetry
dictated by the theory and represented by the analytical results, while other have an asymmetry
in the magnitude of the peaks. The asymmetry seems to increase with the lap length and be
most pronoun for the thinner adhesive layer. It is noticed that the largest peak is in the end
where the load is applied. The values for the maximum stresses for the diﬀerent plots are shown
in table 3.22, where the deviations between the numerical and analytical results are also given.
While the plots only represent the analytical results from the second approach, the table also
includes the maximum values from the ﬁrst approach, presented in section 2.5.
In ﬁgure 3.58 and 3.59, the distribution of the out-of-plane stresses from the Finite Element
Analysis (FEA) is shown. As the analytical value of the maximum out-of-plane stress is directly
dependent on the maximum shear stress, the formulations found during the theoretical studies,
do not give an expression for the distribution of the out-of-plane stress. However, the distribution
for the longer lap lengths, carry great resemblance to that found in other studies, such as [C4].
These distributions are characteristic by zero stresses around the middle, as the shear strength
for these lap lengths. Towards the edge, the out-of-plane stress experiences a slight bulk before
a dominant peak of opposite sign, at the edge. It is possible that for the short lap lengths, the
mesh is too coarse to allow the stresses to form the right stress distribution, while it could also
be possible that the behaviour in the short lap lengths in reality is more chaotic. It is deﬁnitely
interesting to note, that the deviation between the analytical and numerical maximum stresses

with the conﬁguration with a lap length of 25 mm as an exception. This indicates that both
approaches give safe side estimates.
For all the conﬁgurations, the second approach, gives
signiﬁcantly preciser shear stresses, while the ﬁrst approach consequently estimates the shear
stress at least 30% too high and often as much as 80% above.
This corresponds with the
observation in section 2.5, where it was derived, that the ﬁrst approach will typically estimate a
value 1.25-2.0 times higher than the second approach. As was also found in section 2.5, the ﬁrst
approach estimates the out-of-plane stress lower than the ﬁrst approach, relative to the shear

stress. This results in the ﬁrst approach having signiﬁcantly better precision in estimating the

the other hand gives results of relative good precision, especially for thinner adhesive layer. It
can therefore be concluded that this investigation has veriﬁed that the second approach based
on [C4] gives analytical formulations that represent the distribution of the shear stress within
the adhesive layer well and gives good results in regards to both the maximum shear stress
and out-of-plane stress in the adhesive layer. The investigations performed here, show that
imprecisions that might exist in the analytical formulation tend to be on the safe side, unless
the lap length is very short. As a result of this, the second approach will be used to determine
the stresses in the following connection design of an adhesive connection.
In regards to modelling an adhesive connection using ﬁnite element programs, the experience
from this investigation has shown that a very ﬁne mesh is required in order to obtain convergence
in regards to the stress peaks that occur at the edges of the adhesive layers. Even for this simple
2D model of a double lap joint, where options for modelling symmetries by boundary condition
to save computational power are utilized, the limits of the available Abaqus license, as well
==PAGE:118==
Chapter 3. Structural Analysis
104
as the power of the computer, allowing reasonably long computation time, where reached. To
obtain precise results of an adhesive connection, it is therefore neccessary to largely simplify the
layout of the joint, and utilize complex meshing, to obtain local precision of points of interest,
such as the edge of the adhesive layer. Due to this, it is very valuable to understand the usage
of the existing analytical formulations, which are found to be very good indicators. By using
these, it will be possible to make reasonably precise predictions of the capacity of the adhesive
joint, allowing complex ﬁnite element analysis or physical experiments to be used merely as
veriﬁcation of the analytical results, to ensure the required safety. However, relying purely on
the analytical results is not advised, as they are based on simple conﬁguration such as the double
lap joint, and many sources, such as [C4] and [D5], also state that the analytical calculation
shall be veriﬁed by either numerical or physical experiments.
Based on the reasoning given above, the following calculation of adhesive joint will be done
using the analytical formulations, veriﬁed in this subsection, with a stress distribution based on
approach 2. The designed joints will thus be simpliﬁed into double lap joints and the resulting
stresses will be combined when relevant, keeping an lower bound approach in mind. As the
results will be veriﬁed by physical experiments in the experimental research done parallel to
the work of this report, this is found to be reasonable, and will better illustrate some methods
and principles relevant for the scope of this thesis, than proceeding with an advanced 3D ﬁnite
elements model would.
3.5.2
Adhesive Connection Design and Capacity Determination
Figure 3.60: Adhesive connection design concept.
A connection design for the adhesive connection similar to the bolted connection 3, as shown on
ﬁgure 3.60, is proposed. This connection is based on a diagonal cut of the GFRP proﬁles and
otherwise keeping the proﬁles intact. Between the ribs inside the closed proﬁle, angle proﬁles
==PAGE:119==
Chapter 3. Structural Analysis
105
are placed, ﬁtting into the gaps. These are glued to both the MD-plank and the strip proﬁle.
Practically, this could be done, by ﬁrst gluing the angles to the MD-plank allowing the needed
pressure to be applied on the connection while the glue hardens. Following this the strip is glued
to the MD-plank as well as the now ﬁxed angles, allowing good control over the gluing process
and distribution of the glue, in comparison to an approach, where the MD-plank and strip are
already glued together and the angles then pushed into the gap, where the friction between
the angle and the proﬁles will greatly inﬂuence the distribution of the glue. For the connection
to function as intended, the thickness of the angle proﬁles must be performed very precisely,
to allow the plate to have good contact to the angles as well as the MD-plank. Alternative
a expansive glue could be used, to ensure any gaps to be ﬁlled out. This option is decided
against as expansive glues do not have as good mechanical properties as 2-part epoxy, such as
Sikadur330, does.
In the connection in ﬁgure 3.60, a force distribution must be found which is admissible by
ensuring equilibrium in all the angle proﬁles. The forces acting in the connection is shown in
ﬁgure 3.61. The magnitude of these forces was shown earlier in table 3.15.
The vertical and horizontal shear forces are assumed evenly distributed to all the 5 angle proﬁles,
while the moment is distributed as a 45 degree inclined force acting in the middle of the part of
each proﬁle within the horizontal and vertical member, respectively. The restrain of the forces
being inclined by 45 degree, is in order to ensure equilibrium within the angles, by keeping the
connection symmetric around the cut line.
Figure 3.61: Forces acting on connection.
==PAGE:120==

The veriﬁcation of the bearing capacity of the connection will be performed based on these
two force component, by simplifying the connection into two double lap joints, subject to the
horizontal and vertical force component, respectively. The calculations are performed using
the equations presented in section 2.5 as approach 2, and veriﬁed by comparison to FEM in
subsection 3.5.1.
The width of the double lap joints and overlap length is dictated by the
geometry of the connection.
The connection between the web of the proﬁle and the angle
with the applied loads, are illustrated in ﬁgure 3.63. The simpliﬁcation into double lap joints
is illustrated on ﬁgure 3.64 for the horizontal force component, being a x-z elevation of the
isometric view of ﬁgure 3.63. The other lap joint is illustrated in ﬁgure 3.65 for the vertical
force component, being the y-z elevation of the isometric view of ﬁgure 3.63. The width of the
double lap joints will be identical to the lap length of the other joint, meaning that the joint
for the horizontal force component has a width of 95 mm and the joint for the vertical force
component has a width of 405 mm. These 405 mm are taken conservatively, by disregarding
the corner part of the angle. Figures 3.64 and 3.65 are based on the situation for the beam
member of the frame, while the column member has the vertical component where the beam
has the horizontal and vice versa.
Figure 3.63: Isometric view of the angle connection to the web of the proﬁles for the beam
member.
The distribution of the shear stresses occurring in the two diﬀerent lap joint conﬁgurations
are shown for the beam in ﬁgure 3.66 and for the column in ﬁgure 3.67. It is seen that the
distributions are highly unsymmetrical, which is due to the connection being unbalance. This
means, that the combined stiﬀness of the outer plates of the double lap joint, is not equal to that
of the inner plate. This is generally undesirable, as the stresses becomes less evenly distributed.
==PAGE:122==
Chapter 3. Structural Analysis
108
Figure 3.64: Illustration of double lap joint for horizontal force component for beam member
of frame. Width of double lap joint is 95 mm
Figure 3.65: Illustration of double lap joint for vertical force component for beam member of
frame. Width of double lap joint is 405 mm
However, in this case, the geometry dictates that the inner proﬁle must be thicker than the
outer plates, in order to ﬁll out the gap in the proﬁles. The stress distribution shown in ﬁgure
3.66, left, and 3.66, right, corresponds to the double lap joint shown in ﬁgure 3.64. These stress
distributions gives signiﬁcantly higher stresses, which is due to a shorter width (95 mm), while
the increased lap length only contributes to a certain extent. The stress distributions shown in
ﬁgure 3.66, right, and 3.66, left, correspond to the double lap joint of ﬁgure 3.65. This joint
has a short lap length, which would beneﬁt from being further increased, which is seen by the
stress in the middle not reaching zero. The distribution for this lap joint is more unsymmetrical
than the other, which is due to this being dependent on the E-modulus perpendicular to the
pultrusion direction of the outer plates. The inner plate is assumed made by several layers of
GFRP with changing orientation as a plywood, and therefore does not have the same reduction
in stiﬀness, making the joint more unbalanced.

The double lap joint of ﬁgure 3.64 gives shear stresses in
the xz-plane, while the double lap joint of ﬁgure 3.65 gives shear stresses in the zy-plane.
Meanwhile, both double lap joints gives out-of-plane stresses along the z-axis. These stresses
must be combined. To illustrate the stresses in an element within the adhesive joint, the diﬀerent
stresses have been indicated as for an element in the adhesive layer in ﬁgure 3.68. As the out-
of-plane stresses in both the double lap joints that the connection is simpliﬁed into, act in the
z-direction, the out-of-plane stresses can simply be superposed. The shear stresses on the other
hand, act in diﬀerent planes, and must be combined by projection into a critical shear plane
between the xz- and yz-plane. As a simpliﬁed approach of this projection, the two shear stresses
are combined by Pythagoras, giving a total shear distribution as illustrated in 3D in ﬁgure 3.69
==PAGE:124==

for the beam part of the connection. The variation is principally the same for the column part.
In the 3D plot of the stresses, it is clear that the main variation is along the x-axis, as the
component from τxz is dominant.
Figure 3.68: Illustration of stresses acting in adhesive joint.
Figure 3.69: 3D plot of shear stresses in adhesive layer of beam joint.
The maximum stresses acting in the adhesive connection are summarised in table 3.24. Here
both the shear and out-of-plane stresses are shown acting in the adhesive layer as well as within
the GFRP proﬁles. As the depth of the GFRP where the stresses are investigated, conservatively
is chosen as zero, the shear stress in the adhesive layer and in the GFRP element are the same.
The out-of-plane stresses are however not identical. This is due to the out-of-plane stress peaks
in the centre of the adhesive layer, and therefore is reduced along the thickness of the adhesive
layer, as explained in section 2.5.
From table 3.24 it is clear that the critical member is the column, where all the four diﬀerent
relevant stresses are largest. Veriﬁcation of the capacity will therefore be performed based on
the stresses of the adhesive connection in the bottom angle of this member. For the veriﬁcation

As stated in section 2.5, the capacity will be controlled based on two diﬀerent approaches, being
the one presented in [D5], which relates to the capacity of the actual adhesive layer, expecting
failure to occur by rupture of the glue. The other approach is the one presented in [C4], in
which experiments showed that failure would occur by delamination of the GFRP members and
therefore was based on the out-of-plane capacity of these. According to [C4], failure would not
occur due to the capacity of the adhesive being reach, showing a conﬂict in the two approaches.

The result of equation (3.17) show that according to the second approach, the capacity is only
slightly utilized. Meanwhile the veriﬁcation based on the ﬁrst approach shows failure in both
the adhesive and the adherent.
The two approaches hereby give very diﬀerent estimates of
the capacity of the adhesive connection. A main reason for this diﬀerence is, that the second
approach increases the material strengths, with the κ-factors, while the ﬁrst approach uses the
strength directly, as described in section 2.5. The reason why failure in the adherent is so critical
in the ﬁrst approach compared to the second, is also that the ﬁrst approach veriﬁes the capacity
of the adherent based on the out-of-plane tension in the adhesive layer and not in the adherent.
In the adherent, the out-of-plane tension is greatly reduced, as seen in table 3.24. The two
approaches remain conﬂicting, as the second approach claims that failure will always occur in
the adherent due to delamination, while this investigation shows, that failure is also very likely
to happen in the adhesive layer due to the shear capacity according to the ﬁrst approach.
In the investigation above, it was conservatively assumed that the angle is only glued to the
webs of the proﬁles, as illustrated in ﬁgure 3.70, left. This was chosen as it was estimated, that
while a glued connection to the webs could be distributed and be compressed during hardening,
a glued connection to the ribs would be diﬃcult to execute and control, and that applying
compression would not be possible in an eﬀective way. However, if the situation shown in ﬁgure
3.70, right, is chosen, where the angle is also glued to the ribs, the width of the critical lap joint
shown in ﬁgure 3.64 could be increased by the depth of the angle proﬁle, being 36 mm. Taking
this into account, the stresses of table 3.25 are found.

eﬀect. With the force distribution for the adhesive connection, it is found that the angle slightly
