capacity. This is calculated using the same method as for the bolted connection 3 in subsection
3.4.4.
An investigation of the capacity of the adhesive connection has here been performed, which has
revealed a very interesting conﬂict between the approaches given in [D5] and [C4], which disagree
on what part of the connection is relevant to check the capacity of, given very diﬀerent results,
in regards to the utilization of the connection. Both [D5] and [C4] agree that the approaches
they give for analytical calculations of the capacity of the connection should only be indicative
and either advanced numerical or physical experiment should be performed of a connection.
==PAGE:128==
Chapter 3. Structural Analysis
114
To verify the results found here, and to give a better insight in which part of the connection
is critical, a full scale experiment is therefore performed on the connection presented in this
section. A description of this experiment is found in the experimental report.
3.6
Sub conclusion for the structural analysis
In this chapter, a structural analysis has been performed on one of the regular frames, forming
the basis for the structural system of the house used as the case study. The analysis of the
frame started with a determination of the largest sectional forces and deﬂections acting on the
frame for 8 diﬀerent investigated proﬁles. Based on a study of the capacity of these 8 proﬁles in
regards to the applied loads, one of the proﬁles were found more suitable than the others. The
proﬁle which was decided upon, was a MD plank with an added 6 mm strip proﬁle, both being
standard proﬁles from Fiberline. The cross-section of this chosen proﬁles is shown in ﬁgure 3.71.
This was chosen as it had suﬃcient load bearing capacity and a stiﬀness which kept deﬂections
within an acceptable level. Furthermore it was the second lightest proﬁle which reached the
requirement, and was chosen over the lightest, as it had a less dense internal rib structure and
a thicker web, which both are considered would be an advantage in the following connection
design.
Figure 3.71: Adhesive connection design concept I.
The connection design was initiated with an investigation of 4 diﬀerent bolted connection de-
signs. The investigation of the bolted connection showed a signiﬁcant advantage by using a
more complex model for the bolt distribution which takes variation of the moduli of elasticity
into account. Based on the quantitative values for the diﬀerent connection, it could not be
==PAGE:129==
Chapter 3. Structural Analysis
115
determined which was preferred, as choosing a design with less bolts, resulted in additional
need for gusset plates or advanced cutting of the proﬁles. The choice of connection therefore
fell on the architecturally preferred solution, consisting of internal angle proﬁles and a diagonal
cut of the proﬁles. The same connection design was used for a study of the capacity of an
adhesive connection. This study started with a FEM veriﬁcation of the methods intended to be
used, which showed good compliance between the analytical and numerical results. The study
of the capacity of the adhesive connection showed an interesting conﬂict between two applied
approaches, one based on failure of the adhesive and the other on delamination of the GFRP.
The study showed that failure of the adhesive based on the ﬁrst approach will occur, when
the capacity from the second approach is only slightly utilized. This being despite experiments
performed in [C4] show that delamination always will be critical. It is therefore suspected that
the ﬁrst approach greatly underestimates the capacity, but to be on the safe side, the design
was performed so both approaches showed suﬃcient capacity. The connection concept of the
chosen bolted and adhesive connections are shown in ﬁgure 3.72
Figure 3.72: Concepts of chosen connections.
Based on the results from this chapter, experiments to verify these results is performed in the
experimental work done parallel to the work of this report. The description and results of these
experiments are shown in the experimental report. The experiment are carried out using the
proﬁle and connection designs found to be preferred in this chapter.
==PAGE:130==
Chapter 4
Discussion
During the analysis, a structural system of GFRP was presented, which was based upon a series
of regular frames around which internal and external cladding and some special variations were
added, to give the desired geometry of the case structure. While the entire structural system
and strategy was presented, only the regular frames were analysed in detail. Diﬀerent structural
proﬁles were considered for these frames and the best suited was determined to be used in the
design. Based on this proﬁle, solutions for a bolted and a glued connection of the frame corners
were designed and again the most suitable was chosen to be part of the ﬁnal frame design. The
designed structural system was restrained by being constructed purely of standard proﬁles from
Fiberline Composites.
In this chapter, the design solution for the frame will be evaluated in order to determine whether
it can be regarded as a structural eﬃcient system. Following this some suggestions for improve-
ments and optimizations will be presented, disregarding the restrain of standard proﬁles, assum-
ing that new proﬁles could be developed. This could become relevant if the case structure, being
a prototype of a potential building system, proves to meet its ambitions and becomes regarded
as an interesting alternative to traditional detached housing. If the demand is large enough,
new tools for producing such proﬁles would be proﬁtable to develop. Finally, this chapter will
give some general recommendations for structural design of GFRP elements as well as some
considerations that should be made, based on the experience gather through the work of this
report.
Before proceeding with this, a short section concerning the implementation of the structural
system designed in the analysis chapter will be presented.
116
==PAGE:131==
Chapter 4. Discussion
117
4.1
Implementation of the developed structural system
The analysis chapter resulted in a frame design which is structurally feasible, and able to
withstand the expected loads. Even when considering this frame isolated, there are still several
challenges that remain, which are related to production, transport and safety assurance. This
is particularly the case for the connections. Both the bolted and adhesive connections are based
on internal angle proﬁles. For the test elements used for the experimental work of this theses,
these angles were made by cutting out the angles of pultruded plates of 6 mm thickness and
gluing them together with alternating direction of pultrusion, to get equal parameters in both
directions. This method is possible in a production scale aimed for research and possibly for a
single structure, but will not be cost-eﬃcient in larger production of a potential building system.
It is likely that it would be more cost-eﬃcient to make these angles through an other method
than pultrusion, for example by injection moulding, if appropriate mechanical properties can be
assured. Especially in the case of the adhesive connection, further challenges also lie in relation
to establishing the right glue facility on or near the site, or alternatively in transport of the
10 m long and 4 m tall frames. A solution here could be to have a hybrid solution with the
angles glued to the beam and bolted to the column on site. Here the design for the connection
performed in the analysis chapter could still be implemented.
Another challenge of the adhesive connection lies in safety, where it must be demonstrated that
the connection is performed in a satisfactory manner every time, so that suﬃcient capacity is
acquired. It must also be controlled how the adhesive performs through the lifetime of the
structure, to be certain that the mechanical properties remain and it does not degrade due
to moisture, temperature or other inﬂuences. It shall be noted that the adhesive used for the
design here, Sikadur330, has a service temperature between -40 ◦C to 50 ◦C, according to the
datasheet (see appendix H). As parts of the building can easily reach higher temperature when
exposed to sun, the eﬀect of high temperature exposure must be investigated further.
These challenges are all some which must be addressed in relation to the construction of the case
structure. This shall however not be seen as arguments for using conventional solutions instead
or avoiding the use of adhesive connection due to these challenges and limited knowledge on
the lifetime performance. On the contrary, the challenges and limited knowledge demonstrates
the importance such a case structure could potentially have, as it forces solutions to be created
and experience to be gathered, also over the long period of the structures lifetime. Only by
challenging the limits of current knowledge and implementing new methods can these become
==PAGE:132==
Chapter 4. Discussion
118
accepted. However, for the scope of this thesis, all these challenges cannot be addressed and
instead the solutions found for the structural system will in the following be evaluated to identify
the experiences that this has given and where it can be improved.
4.2
Evaluation of the eﬃciency of the developed structural sys-
tem
In structural design of GFRP it is usually found that the SLS restrictions for deﬂections becomes
critical, not allowing the high strength in the direction of pultrusion to be utilized. In relation
to this, most structural design of GFRP can be regarded as ineﬃcient, as the strong properties
of the material are not utilized. As the material remains an expensive alternative to many of
the traditional building materials, such as steel, wood and concrete, this lack of utilization will
often result in the material choice of GFRP to be disadvantageous from a structural perspective.
In the case structure investigated in chapter 3, the choice of GFRP was based upon an ar-
chitectural vision of using the possible translucent property of the material to give an unique
expression. This is combined with the inherent architectural quality of the material, where
the internal structure of the ﬁbres become visible on the surfaces of the building, giving an
alternative to the conventional matt walls and ceilings ﬁnishes. Instead of basing this architec-
tural vision inside an ineﬃcient structural system, it was desired that the structure would also
contribute to tell the story of the possibilities of utilizing the properties of the material. To do
this, the initial challenge was to make the structure functions, but to truly show the advantages
of the material, the structure should avoid underutilization of the material due to dominant
deﬂection limit. The case structure was laid out as a frame structure, as an integrated part
of the architecture as well as being regarded as suitable for a potential building system, where
regular frames could be adapted to countless building geometries.
To minimize deﬂections as well as ensure transverse stability, rigid frames were designed. Using
rigid connections minimizes the deﬂections more than the stresses compared to simple supports.
Furthermore the proﬁles were chosen to be tall and slender, to give a large geometric stiﬀness
in the frames plane, to compensate for the relatively low material stiﬀness. To avoid stability
problems of these tall and slender proﬁles, the external and internal cladding was integrated in
the structural strategy, to restrain the proﬁles from stability problems out of the plane of the
frame. In addition to this, the cladding is utilized to ensure the global stability of the building
==PAGE:133==
Chapter 4. Discussion
119
perpendicular to the plane of the frames. Following this strategy, the most critical utilization
for diﬀerent veriﬁcation criteria are as shown in table 4.1 for the designed frame.
Table 4.1:
Connection comparison.
Veriﬁcation criteria
Utilization
Proﬁle deﬂection
81%
Proﬁle max. shear (long term)
60%
Proﬁle max. shear (short term)
46%
Proﬁle max. normal stress (long term)
33%
Proﬁle max. normal stress (short term)
26%
Bolted connection
86%
Glued connection
100%
It is seen that, as is normally the case for GFRP structures, the deﬂection being the most critical
criteria for the proﬁles. The shear capacity is also relatively utilized, while the normal stress
remains utilized to only a third of the capacity. This utilization of normal stress is furthermore
found for long term loading, which was more critical than short term loading, as the capacity
is signiﬁcantly reduced under long term loading due to the risk of strain rupture. The high
strength of the material, being the capacity for short term normal stress in the direction of
pultrusion, is only 26% utilized. The utilization of the connections are also very high. This is
to a large extent due to them being designed to meet the requirements, but it was found that
additional strengthening will not be easy, as restrictions to maximum bolts in a row is reached
and additional glued area will not largely increase the capacity as the stress peaks will remain
unchanged.
While the designed structural system can be regarded as eﬃcient in regards to all elements
being integrated in the structural strategy to give in the global and local stability, the bearing
capacity of the frame members analysed still are somewhat underutilized. This is especially the
case for the normal stress capacity under short term loading in the direction of pultrusion. This
strength in particular is often used to argue that GFRP has strength properties comparable to
that of steel, as well as an excellent strength-weight ratio. Unless structures are designed, so
that the large strength properties are utilized, a structural system cannot be deemed eﬃcient.
If deﬂections, shear stresses, connection capacity and long term loading end up dominating the
design, the potential of the material is not reached, and it is likely to remain an expensive and
ineﬃcient material choice for structural design.
==PAGE:134==
Chapter 4. Discussion
120
4.3
Design suggestions for optimizing utilization of structural
properties
While being feasible and meeting requirements, the structural system, proﬁles and connection
designed in chapter 3 can not be considered as an optimal structure, and the eﬃciency can be
further improved. As argued above, this statement is due to the capacity of the connections
and requirements for deﬂections are close to the limits, while the largest mechanical strength,
being the short term normal stress capacity in the direction of the pultrusion, is still strongly
underutilized. In the following subsections suggestion for improvements of the structural system,
the proﬁles as well as the connections will be presented.
4.3.1
Improvements for structural system
The strategy of the structural system is viewed as being eﬃcient, using secondary elements,
such as the roof, facade and ﬂoor, both for load distributing and stabilizing the main frames
against buckling and lateral torsional buckling. At the same time, these elements are used to
ensure and increase the global stability. The designed structural system is principally a frame
structure and it could very likely be found that using a principally diﬀerent structure, such as a
shell or truss structures, would lead to a more eﬃcient system. However, changing the principle
for the structural system would require a completely diﬀerent approach and unavoidably change
the design of the house and will therefore not be addressed here.
In the case structure, the long span of the frames is partly the reason for the deﬂections remain
the most critical criteria for the frame members. If for example the span was to be halved,
the deﬂection, disregarding shear contributions, would be reduced by a factor 1/16, due to it
being dependent on l4, while the moment would be reduced by a factor 1/4, being dependent
on l2, and the shear by a factor 1/2. This would result in the bearing capacities becoming more
utilized compared to the deﬂection. However, it would also result in the relatively low shear
strength becoming more critical as well as a larger contribution to the deﬂection from the low
shear modulus, still not allowing an utilization of the high normal stress parallel to the direction
of pultrusion. To get a better balance between the utilization of the shear and normal stress
capacity utilization and reduce the impact of shear deformations, an optimisation of the proﬁles
can be performed by increasing the shear area.
==PAGE:135==
Chapter 4. Discussion
121
4.3.2
Optimization of proﬁles
The proﬁles used in the analysis in chapter 3 was chosen well knowing that none of the in-
vestigated proﬁles were optimal, but were chosen as possible solutions where standard proﬁles
from Fiberline are used, still accommodating the architectural wishes. The geometry of the cho-
sen proﬁles was desired to represent something new, as a counterpart to the typical structural
proﬁles of GFRP. These proﬁles carry great resemblance to steel proﬁles, such as the I-proﬁle,
giving strong associations to steel structures and disregarding that the mechanical properties
are signiﬁcantly diﬀerent. As was found in this analysis, a GFRP proﬁle shall have a stronger
focus on shear resistance, due to a low shear modulus and strength. In order to get larger shear
resistance, a box proﬁle is a possible choice, having two webs to distribute the shear. In ﬁgure
4.1, the proﬁle determined most suitable in the structural analysis is shown, together with a
version without the internal ribs and two proposals for optimized proﬁles. As the internal ribs
neither contribute much to the moment of inertia or to reduce the shear stresses, it was found
of interest to determine how the geometric properties relevant for the structural analysis was
inﬂuenced by removing these ribs.
Figure 4.1: Illustration of determined proﬁle and optimized versions.
As seen in table 4.2, the mass is reduced more than the moment of inertia, given a better ratio
between the two. The web area is also shown, representing the shear area, and thus the proﬁles
resistance to shear. This remains unchanged by removing the ribs, as the impact of the ribs is
insigniﬁcant in regards to the shear distribution. Based on this observation, an optimized proﬁle
is presented, which is dimensioned to have the same external dimensions, moment of inertia and
web area as the proﬁle determined in the analysis.
It is seen in table 4.2 that while these
==PAGE:136==

parameters remain unchanged, resulting in a structural performance of no signiﬁcant diﬀerence,
the mass is reduced by 15%. As the case structure generally is very light, and GFRP has reduced
strength under long term loading, such a reduction could be of signiﬁcance. Furthermore, the
optimized proﬁle beneﬁts from being completely double symmetric and a single proﬁle, which
can be produced directly by pultrusion.
This optimization is performed only based on the
mentioned parameters, and it shall be noted that stability of the web and sensitivity to impact
loading of the thin webs of this optimized proﬁle could be critical. A second optimal proﬁle is
also presented, which contrary to the ﬁrst is restrained to have the same mass as the proﬁle
chosen in the analysis, but increased moment of inertia and especially increased web area. It
is seen that without changing the external dimensions or the mass, it is possible to design a
proﬁle with a 12% increase in moment of inertia and almost 20% increase in web area. This
will results in decreased deﬂection and a larger decrease of the shear stresses than the normal
stresses. Hereby a more even utilization could be accomplished. Furthermore the web thickness
is increased with 10% contributing to the performance of the connections.
This demonstrated two important aspects, being that proﬁles can be optimized from a com-
pletely objective basis and that they can be shaped to better ﬁt a particular structure, to ensure
a more even utilization of the structural elements, and thus a more optimized structure.
This can become an important part of the continuous expansion of the use of GFRP as a struc-
tural material. As new proﬁle ranges continue to be developed, designers are given a larger
choice of proﬁles to ﬁt the speciﬁc structure, resulting in more optimized usage of the proﬁles
ultimately leading to more eﬃcient utilization of the material. As a further aspect of the fu-
ture proﬁle optimization, which truly brings the inherent nature of the material to beneﬁt, one
could imagine structural FRP proﬁles with a hybrid ﬁbre structure. By placing carbon ﬁbres
in the top and bottom of proﬁles the stiﬀness can be greatly increased, as the E-modulus is
signiﬁcantly higher for carbon ﬁbres. Hereby material properties and proﬁle performance could
==PAGE:137==
Chapter 4. Discussion
123
further be customized to the speciﬁc structure.
Combined with geometric variations of the
proﬁle, it could become possible to balance the performance of the proﬁle so truly optimized
proﬁles are acquired, that meet deﬂection and capacity criteria simultaneously. However, as
the market is now, the price of carbon ﬁbres is high, and a large beneﬁt from their use must
therefore be ensured if they are to be used. Nonetheless, the possibility of being able to design
the properties of FRP through the ﬁbre composition has huge potential, and is a area where the
material clearly stands out from other structural materials such as steel and wood, where the
property composition is more ﬁxed. With increased understanding and control over the design
of the mechanical properties combined with the possibility of advanced cross-section shapes
through pultrusion, structures of GFRP can potentially be design to be very eﬃcient.
4.3.3
Suggestions for connection design
The connections were found to be critical, reaching stresses close to the capacity and requiring
large amounts of angle proﬁles and bolts or adhesive. However, this was based on a conservative
restriction that the connection design had to reach suﬃcient capacity veriﬁcation for all the
investigated approaches. This was found neccessary due to the limited design guidance that is
available, and limited veriﬁcation of that which exists. If the adhesive connection was made
purely based on the second approach, the area of the adhesive layer could be greatly reduced. It
is thus possible that much more elegant and cost-eﬃcient adhesive connections could be possible,
as is already strongly indicated through one of the two investigated approaches. The same goes
for the bolted connection, where the capacity was found to be greatly increased if the forces
follow the direction of higher stiﬀness, allowing fewer bolts.
The optimized proﬁle presented above, would make the chosen solution with internal angles
impractical, while some of the considered options with a continuous gusset plate more advan-
tageous. As found in the analysis, continuous gusset plates also has the beneﬁt, that the bolt
forces can distribute more freely and follow the strong direction of pultrusion, which will attract
larger forces due to the higher stiﬀness. However, from the structural and material economical
point of view, an optimized solution would involve no gusset plate, but rather be a connection
directly between the beam and column proﬁles. If the beam is as the optimized proﬁle presented
above and the column has the same internal dimensions as the beams external, cutting away
the top 500 mm of the columns ﬂange, would allow a connection as shown in ﬁgure 4.2. Here
the members can be glued or bolted directly together avoiding the need for gusset plate, and
==PAGE:138==
Chapter 4. Discussion
124
due to the thin webs, the additional thickness of the column would hardly be visible. Such a
bolted joint could take advantage of a circular bolt pattern, similar to the one of connection
4 in the analysis, which utilizes all the bolts similarly. This is especially the case, as in such
a connection the bolts could not follow the direction of pultrusion, as the beam and column
combined would have equal stiﬀness in both directions.
Beam, external thickness 46 mm
Column, external thickness 56 mm,

4.4
Recommendations and considerations for structural design
with GFRP
As the ﬁnal section of this chapter, this section will seek to give some general recommendation
for structural design in GFRP. The recommendations are based on the experienced gained
through both the literature studies and the performed analysis.
Structural design of GFRP elements, should always begin with controlling the deﬂections, as
these are likely to be critical for the design. The design should therefore also be made in order
to reduce the deﬂection. This was here done by rigid connections, which eﬀectively reduce the
deﬂection as well as give stability. If not appropriate, simply supported members should be
attempted to span over two spans, giving larger reductions of deﬂections than both one and
three spans, and as the bearing capacity will not usually be critical, the further reduction of
==PAGE:139==
Chapter 4. Discussion
125
moment and shear gained by three spans is not of much advantage. The design must be carefully
balanced to have span lengths which are suﬃciently short to avoid too large deﬂections as a
results of the low modulus of elasticity, but long enough to avoid a too large contribution from
the low shear modulus. Compared to steel structures, the shear capacity shall be more in focus
when designing GFRP proﬁles, as both shear deﬂections and failure is more likely to occur.
This is done by larger webs areas, possibly making box-proﬁles, as used in this report, favoured
over I-proﬁles from steel.
If rigid bolted connections are designed, a more detailed force distribution, taking the stiﬀness
into account shall always be made when appropriate. The analysis performed here shows that
it can potentially more than double the veriﬁed capacity of the connection.
Adhesive connections could potentially become the preferred way of connecting GFRP structure,
if suﬃciently accurate and simple calculation methods are developed. If an adhesive is developed
that is suﬃciently strong and stiﬀ, so that large stress peaks are avoided and failure does not
occur in the adhesive, rigid connection design could become so eﬃcient, that it will no longer be
critical for the design. In adhesive design, it shall always be attempted to make the lap width
as wide as possible, as this is inversely proportional with the critical stresses. The lap length on
the other side, only beneﬁts the capacity of the connection up to a certain point, after which
a further lap length will simply result in a longer length with zero stresses in the centre of the
lap.
Structural design of GFRP should watch out for long term loading, as this will tend to be more
critical than short term loading, despite lower magnitudes of the forces. This again jeopardized
the ability to utilize the highest strengths. Here the material beneﬁts from its low density,
reducing permanent loading due to its self-weight. It is therefore not beneﬁcial to use GFRP to
carry heavy structures permanently as stress rupture will occur, reducing the capacity.
The structure examined here was chosen to be a frame structure, which harmonized with the
design of the case structure. However, the investigations performed here has shown that for
such a structure it is very diﬃcult to take advantage of the high axial strength of the material.
The material properties could be found more suited for truss and shell structures, where the
axial forces are dominant.
==PAGE:140==
Chapter 5
Conclusion
Structural design with GFRP is challenging due to the lack of existing, commonly recognized
structural codes and missing traditions for how to design structures in order to utilize the
strengths of the material.
The result of this, is that the deﬂection and shear capacity will
become dominant, which can both be considered as weak properties of GFRP. In order to
use GFRP eﬃciently and take advantage of the potential of the material, the strengths of the
material must be utilized, being high axial strength in short term loading and low density.
The work of this report has developed a structurally feasible rigid frame, that operates as a
repetitive element in a case structure. The analysis showed that current design manuals are
suﬃcient for structural member design in regular use, though with limited stress interaction and
stability control. As little guidance can be found on rigid connection design of GFRP, approaches
were proposed based on common force distributions and available veriﬁcation methods for bolted
and adhesive joints. For the bolted connections, great potential was found from detailing a
force distribution in order to take the anisotropic stiﬀness of the material into account. For the
adhesive connection, two approaches were investigated, where one was shown to give a more
precise stress estimation, evaluated based on a FEM. The same approach was found to predict
much larger bearing capacity for the joint, indicating that higher capacity can be veriﬁed for
adhesive joints, if such an approach can be proven accurate and safe.
As is typically the case, deﬂections showed to be critical for the proﬁle design of the frame.
Additionally it was found that long term loading, though signiﬁcantly lower, was more critical
than short term loading. This is due to the phenomenon strain rupture, which causes proﬁles
126
==PAGE:141==
Chapter 6. Conclusion
127
to fail at stresses far lower than the short term capacity. As a result, the high axial strength of
the material in short term loading, was poorly utilized.
Being a composite material with ﬁbres that can principally be varied both in relation to material,
type and orientation, the future possibilities for FRP are large. The properties of GFRP are
somewhat unbalanced for conventional beam design, as the stiﬀness and shear resistance is too
low to probably utilize the high axial strengths. However, more utilized performance could be
possible to acquire with more optimized proﬁles and possible integration of carbon ﬁbres to
increase the E-modulus.
Like all other structural materials, GFRP has disadvantages, which are related to a relatively
high price, low stiﬀness, low shear resistance and reduced strength under long term loading.
Meanwhile, it does also have advantages and possesses interesting properties both for and besides
the structural design. It shall therefore be seen as an integrated part of the repertoire of the
structural engineer, and it can be expected that its signiﬁcance will grow as recognized codes are
developed, calculation methods are detailed, and production continues to expand and improve.
As was shown in this report, it is diﬃcult to design an arbitrary structure so that the strength
of the material is utilized. From a structural perspective, the GFRP should be chosen, when its
composition of properties suits the current conditions. This is when; deﬂection are tolerable,
short term loading is dominant, low density is advantageous and high axial strength is required.
5.1
Suggestions for further research
Structural design of GFRP is a relatively new discipline, strongly demonstrated by the lack of
recognized codes and regulations, such as on implementation in the Eurocode system. Instead
designers are guided by design manuals from the producers of the proﬁles. While these have
been found to be of great assistance in the design, they are limited to common application
of already existing proﬁles. The suggestion for further research within the topic of structural
design of GFRP is therefore almost inexhaustible, as the material is still in its very early stage
of becoming routine structural material.
Based purely on the approaches and applications
examined in this thesis, the following suggestions for further research can be mentioned:
• Study of rigid bolted connection design of GFRP, determining allowable assumptions in
regards to force distribution dependent on stiﬀness and clarifying reductions for allowing
additional bolts in rows and decreasing minimum distances.
==PAGE:142==
Chapter 6. Conclusion
128
• Further veriﬁcation of stress determination in adhesive connections and development of
precise, safe and unambiguous capacity determination taking all relevant failure mech-
anisms into account, as well as interaction of several multi-directed stress parameters.
Additionally, development of design guides for connection of more advanced geometry
than single and double lap joints.
• Increased guidance for controlling local stability of structural elements, such as lateral
torsional buckling, for existing proﬁles.
• Establishment of interaction formulas and veriﬁcation for stress components in structural
proﬁles.
Speciﬁcally concerning the construction of the case structure, following areas would be of interest
and partly neccessary to have researched further:
• Cost and production optimization of the angle proﬁles used in the connection to allow
broader cost-eﬃcient application.
• Construction and control procedures for erection of the frames and veriﬁcation of the
capacity of the construction, especially in relation to adhesive joints.
• Structural design of internal and external cladding, ensuring both local stability of the
frames and global stability of the house. Connection design of these elements to the regular
frames.
• Structural design of the special features of the house, especially of the inclined, cantilevered
end part, carrying the large glass panel. For the conceptional integrity of the structure,
approaches, connections and proﬁles from the regular frame should be implemented.
• Design of appropriate insulation, indoor environment, ﬁre safety and geotechnics to reach
requirements, without compromising the architectural vision.

 58 % F_d(i)=sqrt((F_vd+F_md(i)*AutoCor(i,1)/r(i))^2+(F_hd+F_md(i)*AutoCor(i,2)/r(i))^2);
 59 % % Resulting force acting on bolts per shear plane [N]
 60 % alpha(i)=acos((F_hd+F_md(i)*AutoCor(i,2)/r(i))/F_d(i));
 61 % alpha_deg(i)=alpha(i)*180/pi;
 62 % end
 63 
 64 %
 65 F_md_par=M_d*AutoCor(:,2)/(n_sp*sum(AutoCor(:,2).^2))*10^6;
 66 F_md_Ex=-M_d*AutoCor(:,2)/(n_sp*sum(AutoCor(:,2).^2))*10^6;
 67 F_md_Ey=M_d*AutoCor(:,1)/(n_sp*sum(AutoCor(:,1).^2))*10^6;
 68 
 69 %% Plot
 70 % Plots:
 71 subplot(221)
 72 plot(AutoCor(:,1),AutoCor(:,2),'k*');
 73 hold on
 74 plot(Origo(1),Origo(2),'kX');
 75 Faktor=10^-2; % Scaling factor of forces for plots
 76 for i=1:length(AutoCor)
 77 a=F_md(i)*Faktor;
 78 b=sqrt((AutoCor(i,1)-Origo(1))^2+(AutoCor(i,2)-Origo(2))^2);
 79 Fx(i)=AutoCor(i,1)+a*(Origo(2)-AutoCor(i,2))/b; % change + before -, to change direction
 80 Fy(i)=AutoCor(i,2)+a*(AutoCor(i,1)-Origo(1))/b; % change + before -, to change direction
 81 Fx_E(i)=(AutoCor(i,1)+E_hor/(E_ver+E_hor)*F_md_Ex(i)*Faktor);%+E_ver/(E_ver+E_hor)*Fx(i)
 82 Fy_E(i)=(AutoCor(i,2)+E_ver/(E_ver+E_hor)*F_md_Ey(i)*Faktor);%+E_ver/(E_ver+E_hor)*Fy(i)
 83 
 84 
 85 % plot([Fx(i),AutoCor(i,1)],[Fy(i),AutoCor(i,2)],'b')
 86 Fx_tot(i)=Fx(i)+F_hd*Faktor;
 87 Fy_tot(i)=Fy(i)+F_vd*Faktor;
 88 % plot([Fx_tot(i),AutoCor(i,1)],[Fy_tot(i),AutoCor(i,2)],'r')
 89 
 90 Fx_tot_E(i)=Fx_E(i)+F_hd*Faktor;
 91 Fy_tot_E(i)=Fy_E(i)+F_vd*Faktor;
 92 
 93  plot([Fx_tot_E(i),AutoCor(i,1)],[Fy_tot_E(i),AutoCor(i,2)],'r')
 94 
 95 
 96 P_hor(i)=abs((abs(Fx_tot(i))-abs(AutoCor(i,1))))/Faktor;
 97 P_ver(i)=abs((abs(Fy_tot(i))-abs(AutoCor(i,2))))/Faktor;
 98 
 99 P_hor_E(i)=abs((abs(Fx_tot_E(i))-abs(AutoCor(i,1))))/Faktor;
100 P_ver_E(i)=abs((abs(Fy_tot_E(i))-abs(AutoCor(i,2))))/Faktor;
101 % Source: http://answers.google.com/answers/threadview/id/419874.html
102 %  plot([Fx_tot_E(i),AutoCor(i,1)],[AutoCor(i,2),AutoCor(i,2)],'r')
103 %  plot([AutoCor(i,1),AutoCor(i,1)],[Fy_tot_E(i),AutoCor(i,2)],'b')
104 end
105 
106 % %% Resistance calculations
107 P_BD0=f_cB0*d*t/gamma_m + f_cB90*d*t_2/gamma_m; % [N]
108 P_BD90=f_cB90*d*t/gamma_m + f_cB0*d*t_2/gamma_m;%[N]
109 
110 %% Checks
111 j=1;
112 q=1;

Appendix B
Lisp code to get bolt coordinates
The following Lisp code is used in the report to get the coordinates of bolts from a Autocad
drawing of a connection and then copied into a MatLab-script:
(princ "\ nType
PTF to start ")
(defun c:ptf ()
(setq
FileName (getstring
"\ nEnter
file
name
for
coordinate
output: "))
(setq f1 (open
FileName "w"))
(princ "\ nSelect
circles
to write to file: ")
(setq
PointSet (ssget
’((0 . "CIRCLE "))))
(setq
EntNum 0)
(repeat (sslength
PointSet)
(setq
ename (ssname
PointSet
EntNum ))
(setq
PtCoord (cdr (assoc 10 (entget
ename ))))
(setq
xval (rtos (car
PtCoord) 2 4)
yval (rtos (cadr
PtCoord) 2 4)
zval (rtos (caddr
PtCoord) 2 4)
PtString (strcat
xval "," yval "," zval)
)
(write -line
PtString
f1)
(setq
EntNum
(1+
EntNum ))
)
(close f1)
(princ)
)
The lisp code is found at ’http://www.cadtutor.net/forum/archive/index.php/t-12172.html’.

Appendix E
Inherent architecture of GFRP
This appendix consists of some pages showing the internal ﬁbre composition in the used GFRP
proﬁles, together with pictures illustrating how this internal ﬁbre composition is visible through
the transparency of the material. This visibility of the internal structure is what is referred to
as the inherent architecture of the material. All the pages in this appendix are made by Vicki
Thake.

Appendix G
Loads on structure
Superimposed Dead Load
The roof, fa¸cade and ﬂoor structure with internal cladding, could consists of a double layer of
MD plank proﬁle. This gives following dead weight:
groof,GFRP = 2 · 13.140kg/m2 · 9.81m/s2 = 0.258kN/m2
(G.1)
In addition to this, the roof, ﬂoors and walls will consist of insulation, membranes and some
installations. As these are all relatively light, it is considered reasonable to operate with a dead
load of:
groof = 0.500kN/m2
(G.2)
This superimposed dead load is applied to all the frame members, multiplied by 2 m, being the
distance between the frames.
Snow load
Sk = 0.8kN/m2
(G.3)
161
==PAGE:176==
Appendix G. Loads on structure
162
Live loads
According to the Danish annex of Eurocode 1, the live load is set as:
qk = 1.5kN/m2
(G.4)
For rooms in residential buildings and houses.
Wind load
Terrain Category III:
z0 = 0.3m
zmin = 5m
(G.5)
vb = 24m/s
(G.6)
The basic wind pressure:
qb = 1/2ρv2
b = 360N/m2
(G.7)
Where ρ = 1.25kg/m3. Using ﬁgure 4.2 of EC1-4 for terrain category III, gives ce(z) = 1.29,
giving a peak velocity pressure of:
qp(z) = ce(z)qb = 464.4N/m2
(G.8)
The wind load is divided into following zones:
The external wind pressure is determined by:
we = qb(ze)cpe
(G.9)
The internal wind pressure is determined by:
wi = qb(zi)cpi
(G.10)
With wind from the side of the building, all openings are at negative pressure coeﬃcients, giving
µ = 1 and as h/d ≤0.25, a internal wind coeﬃcient of cpi = 0.3 is determined. This gives a
internal wind pressure of:
wi = 134N/m2
(G.11)

Sikadur

Sikadur®-330
2-Part Epoxy Impregnation Resin
Product 
Description
Sikadur®-330 is a two part, solvent free, thixotropic epoxy based impregnating 
resin / adhesive.
Uses
	 Impregnation resin for SikaWrap® fabric reinforcement for the dry application 
method
	 Primer resin for the wet application system
	 Structural adhesive for bonding Sika® CarboDur® plates to even surfaces
Characteristics / 
Advantages
	 Easy mix and application by trowel and impregnation roller
	 Manufactured for manual saturation methods
	 Excellent application behaviour to vertical and overhead surfaces
	 Good adhesion to many substrates
	 High mechanical properties
	 No separate primer required
	 Solvent free
Tests
Approval / Standards
Conforms to the requirements of:
	 SOCOTEC (France): Cahier des charges Sika® CarboDur, SikaWrap®.
	 Road and Bridges Research Institute (Poland): IBDiM No AT/2003-04-336.

Form

==PAGE:181==
Application Details
Consumption
This will be dependant on the roughness of the substrate and the type of 
SikaWrap® fabric to be impregnated. See respective SikaWrap® fabric Product 
Data Sheet. 
Guide: 0.7 - 1.5 kg/m2
Substrate Quality
The substrate must be sound and of sufficient tensile strength to provide a 
minimum pull off strength of 1.0 N/mm2 or as per the requirements of the design 
specification.
The surface must be dry and free of all contaminants such as oil, grease, coatings 
and surface treatments etc.
The surface to be bonded must be level (max. deviation 2 mm per 0.3 m length), 
with steps and formwork marks not greater than 0.5 mm. High spots can be 
removed by abrasive blasting or grinding.
Wrapped corners must be rounded to a minimum radius of 20 mm (depending 
on the SikaWrap® fabric type) or as per the design specification. This can be 
achieved by grinding edges or by building up with Sikadur® mortars.
Substrate Preparation
Concrete and masonry substrates must be prepared mechanically using 
abrasive blast cleaning or grinding equipment, to remove cement laitance, loose 
and friable material to achieve a profiled open textured surface.
Timber substrates must be planed or sanded.
All dust, loose and friable material must be completely removed from all surfaces 
before application of the Sikadur®-330 preferably by brush and industrial vacuum 
cleaner. Weak concrete/masonry must be removed and surface defects such as 
honeycombed areas, blowholes and voids must be fully exposed.
Repairs to substrate, filling of blowholes/voids and surface levelling must be 
carried out using Sikadur®-41 LP or a mixture of Sikadur®-30 LP and Sikadur®-
501 quartz sand (mix ratio 1 : 1 max parts by weight).
Bond tests must be carried out to ensure substrate preparation is adequate.
Inject cracks wider than 0.25 mm with Sikadur®-52 LP or other suitable Sikadur® 
injection resin.
Application 
Conditions / Limitations
Substrate Temperature 
+10°C min. / +35°C max.
Ambient Temperature
+10°C min. / +35°C max.
Substrate Humidity
< 4% moisture content. Test method: Sika-Tramex meter.
Dew Point
Beware of condensation!
Ambient temperature during application must be at least 3°C above dew point.
Application Instructions
Mixing
Part A : part B = 4 : 1 by weight 
When using bulk material the exact mixing ratio must be safeguarded by 
accurately weighing and dosing each component.
	
Sikadur®-330	
3/6
Appendix H. Sikadur330
167
==PAGE:182==
Mixing Time
Pre-batched units: Mix parts A+B together for at least 3 
minutes with a mixing spindle attached to a slow speed 
electric drill (max. 600 rpm) until the material becomes 
smooth in consistency and a uniform grey colour. Avoid 
aeration while mixing. Then, pour the whole mix into a clean 
container and stir again for approx. 1 more minute at low 
speed to keep air entrapment at a minimum. Mix only that 
quantity which can be used within its potlife.
Bulk packing, not pre-batched:First, stir each part thoroughly. 
Add the parts in the correct proportions into a suitable mixing pail 
and stir correctly using an electric low speed mixer as above for 
pre-batched units.
Application Method / 
Tools
Preparation:
Prior to application confirm substrate moisture content, 
relative humidity and dew point.
Cut the specified SikaWrap® fabric to the desired 
dimensions.
Resin Application:
Apply the Sikadur®-330 to the prepared substrate using a 
trowel, roller or brush.
Fabric Placement and Laminating:
Place the SikaWrap® fabric in the required direction onto the 
Sikadur®-330. Carefully work the fabric into the resin with the 
Sika plastic impregnation roller parallel to the fiber direction 
until the resin is squeezed out between and through the fiber 
strands and distributed evenly over the whole fabric surface. 
Avoid excessive force when laminating to prevent folding or 
creasing of the SikaWrap® fabric. 
Additional Fabric Layers:
For additional layers of SikaWrap® fabric, apply Sikadur®-
330 to previous applied layer wet on wet within 60 minutes 
(at +23°C) after application of the previous layer and repeat 
laminating procedure. 
If it is not possible to apply within 60 minutes, a waiting time 
of at least 12 hours must be observed before application of 
next layer.
Overlays:
If a cementitious overlay is to be applied over SikaWrap® 
fabric an additional Sikadur-330 resin layer must be applied 
over final layer at a max. 0.5 kg/m2. Broadcast with quartz 
sand while wet which will serve as a key for the overlay. 
If a coloured coating is to be applied the wet Sikadur®-330 
surface can be smoothed with a brush.
Overlaps
Fiber Direction:
	 Overlapping of the SikaWrap® fabric must be at least 
100 mm (depending on the SikaWrap® fabric type) or as 
specified in the strengthening design.
Side by Side:
	 Unidirectional fabrics: when placing several unidirectional 
SikaWrap® fabrics side by side no overlapping is required 
unless specified in the strengthening design.
	 Multi-directional fabrics: overlapping in the weft direction 
must be at least 100 mm (depending on the SikaWrap 
fabric type) or as specified in the strengthening design. 
Cleaning of Tools
Clean all equipment immediately with Sika® Colma Cleaner. Cured material can 
only be mechanically removed.
	
Sikadur®-330	
4/6

Potlife
Potlife:
Temperature
Time
+10°C
90 minutes (5 kg)
+35°C
30 minutes (5 kg)
Potlife starts with the mixing of both parts (resin and hardener). At low ambient 
temperature pot life will be extended, at elevated temperatures this will be 
reduced. The higher the quantity of material mixed, the shorter the potlife. To 
achieve a longer potlife at high temperatures the mixed material may be divided 
into smaller units or both parts may be cooled before mixing.
Open time:
Temperature
Time
+10°C
60 minutes
+35°C
30 minutes

Times are approximate and will be affected by changing ambient conditions.
Notes on Application / 
Limitations
This product may only be used by experienced professionals.
The Sikadur®-330 must be protected from rain for at least 24 hours after 
application.
Ensure placement of fabric and laminating with roller takes place within open 
time.
The SikaWrap® fabric must be coated with a cementitious overlay or coating 
for aesthetic and/or protective purposes. Selection will be dependent on 
exposure requirements. For basic UV protection use Sikagard®-550W Elastic, 
Sikagard® ElastoColor-675W or Sikagard®-680S.
At low temperatures and / or high relative humidity, a tacky residue (blush) may 
form on the surface of the cured Sikadur-330 epoxy. If an additional layer of 
fabric, or a coating is to be applied onto the cured epoxy, this residue must first 
be removed to ensure adequate bond. The residue can be removed with water. 
In both cases, the surface must be wiped dry prior to application of the next layer 
or coating.
For application in cold or hot conditions, pre-condition material for 24 hours in 
temperature controlled storage facilities to improve mixing, application and pot 
life limits.
The number of additional fabric layers applied wet on wet must be closely 
controlled to avoid creeping, creasing or slippage of the fabric during curing of the 
Sikadur®-330. The number of layers will be dependent on the type of SikaWrap® 
fabric used and the ambient climate conditions.

Chapter 1
Introduction
Structural design of glass ﬁbre reinforced polymer (GFRP) is a relatively new discipline, and
while veriﬁcation of the capacity of the structural members is well established, the connection
design is less developed. This especially goes for rigid connections, both for bolted and adhesive
joints. For rigid bolted connections, some researchers believe that the inherently low stiﬀness
of the material will result in the connections only acting as semi-rigid connections ([A1]). For
adhesive connection design, analytical formulations only represents simple single and double lap
joints, and even for these, it is advised that results are veriﬁed by experiments ([C1], [D1]).
This Experimental Report is to be read in connection with the Main Report, where a rigid frame
is designed based on presented theory. A major part of the structural design of the rigid frame
was the connections, which were designed both as bolted connections and adhesive connections.
Due to the uncertainties presented above, it is decided to perform an experimental veriﬁcation
of the capacity of the designed connections. These experiments of the bolted corner connection
and the adhesive corner connection are presented in this report. In addition to this, three of the
internal angle proﬁles are tested separately, to ensure that these have the expected capacity.
Due to the limited amount of test specimens, the veriﬁcation done here can only be regarded
as indicative, as variations in material properties, assembly of the connection and uncertainty
in measurements could give variation from connection to connection, possibly making the test
results unrepresentative. In order to determine a reliable capacity of the connections and un-
derstand the variations that occur, more test should be performed.
It shall be noted, that due to delays in commencing the experiments, only the tests of the three
angle proﬁles have been completed for the submission of the thesis. The results of the corner
connections are therefore not given here, but the preparations, creation of the test specimens,
test setup and expected results are given. This is done with an expectancy that the results from
the corner connection can be presented at the oral presentation of this thesis.
1
==PAGE:193==
Chapter 2
Theory
As this Experimental Report is to be read in connection with the Main Report, the work within
this report relies on the theory presented in the Main Report. Theory concerning GFRP or the
approaches used to determine the capacity will therefore not be given here. The only theory
that will be presented is connected to the experiments and the expected capacities. As the
characteristic material properties given for GFRP are based on the lower 5% fractile, it is likely
that failure will happen above the characteristic value of the material.
In the following, a
method will be presented to estimate approximately how much larger the mean capacity will
be compared to the characteristic values.
2.1
Estimation of mean material strength
The strength properties used in the work of this thesis are based on values from Fiberline’s
design manual ([D4]) and are characteristics values. These value are, as in Eurocode, based on
the lower 5% fractile of the material strength. This is illustrated in ﬁgure 2.1. This plot is for
concrete specimens and assumes a normal distribution. Assuming that the properties of GFRP
likewise follow a normal distribution, the relation between the characteristic value and the mean
value can, in accordance with [D2], be determined by:
rk = rm −1.64SD
(2.1)
Where, rk is the characteristic strength property, rm is the mean of strength property and SD
is the standard deviation. The determination of the partial safety factor should according to
[B1] be based on an aim of providing a probability of failure below the design strength of 10−3.
This is fulﬁlled when the design resistance is 3.04 times the standard deviation, SD, away from
2
==PAGE:194==
Chapter 1. Introduction
3
Figure 2.1: Illustration of material strength distribution and characteristic strength (from
[D2]).
the mean strength, rm, in the normal distribution:
rd = rm −3.04SD
(2.2)
The relation between the characteristic value and the design value is known, being the safety
coeﬃcient of γGFRP = 1.3 for the GFRP elements, as all tests will be short term loading, and
γAdhesive = 3.0 for the adhesive. The magnitude of the mean strength can thus be estimated
based on the characteristic strength by solving equation (2.1) and (2.2). This gives the estimated
mean strength values shown in table 2.1
Table 2.1: Relation between design, characteristic and estimated mean strength
Design strength
Characteristic strength
Estimated mean strength
fd
fk
fm
GFRP
fk/1.3
1.0fk
1.27fk
Adhesive
fk/3.0
1.0fk
1.78fk
The values in the table give an indication of how much larger the mean strength will be com-
pared to the characteristic strength. In principal, it is most probable that the test specimens
can withstand the applied load that corresponds to the hardest loaded part of the test specimen
being loaded until its estimated mean strength. However, the estimation of the mean strength
here is somewhat theoretical, assuming a perfect normal distribution and that the safety coef-
ﬁcient is determined to give a failure below the design value with a probability of 10−3. The
estimated mean strength shall therefore only be seen as indicative, in order to give an under-
standing and quantiﬁcation of why it is expected that failure is experienced signiﬁcantly above
==PAGE:195==
Chapter 1. Introduction
4
the characteristic or design strength. It is also likely to obtain higher capacities due to the
design being carried out based on a lower bound theorem, striving to make simpliﬁcation to
result in a safer design.
In the following chapter the preparations done for the experiments is presented, including the
test setup, creation of the test specimens, as well as a calculation of the expected capacities
from the test. These expected capacities will result in an estimation of the applied failure load
when using the design strength, characteristic strength and estimated mean strength, presented
in this section. Hereby, it can be evaluated to what extent the actual failure loads from the
experiments vary from the expectancy and whether these failure loads are unexpectedly low or
high. The capacity is also calculated here due to some minor changes in the geometry of the
actual test specimen compared to the designs presented and calculated in the Main Report,
resulting in some variation in the expected capacity.
==PAGE:196==
Chapter 3
Experimental preparations
In this chapter the preparations done for the experiments are presented. Three diﬀerent elements
have been tested, being the internal angle proﬁles used in the corner connections, the bolted
corner connection and the adhesive corner connection. For each of these three types of test
specimens, the test setup is explained, followed by the creation and ﬁnal geometry of the test
specimens, as well as the expected capacity of the actual test specimens, calculated based on
the design, characteristic and estimated mean capacity.
3.1
Angle proﬁles
Three separate angle proﬁles, identical to the ones used in the corner connections, were created
to be tested separately. This was to ensure that the capacity of these internal angles was as
expected, whereby a possible weakness in the connection could be identiﬁed.
3.1.1
Test setup
The angle proﬁles are tested through a tension test, pulling in the ends of the proﬁle, creating
a combined moment, axial and shear force in the corner of the angle proﬁle where failure is
expected to occur.
The angle proﬁle is attached to a fork proﬁle of steel, connected to the test machine and bolted
to the angle proﬁle, as illustrated in ﬁgure 3.1. The force is applied by a test machine with a
capacity of 100 kN.
5
==PAGE:197==
Chapter 2. Experimental preparations
6
Figure 3.1: Principle for test setup for angle proﬁle.
3.1.2
Creating the test specimens
The angle proﬁles were created at Fiberline Composites, by cutting out the angle proﬁle of 6
mm thick strips in the complete shape of the angle proﬁle and then gluing them together with
alternating directions of pultrusion. The strips were glued together using Sikadur330, as for
the adhesive connection. The properties of this glue are described in the Main Report, and
the datasheet is attached there as an appendix. The result is a proﬁle with equal mechanical
properties around both its axes, symmetrical around the corner of the proﬁle. One side of the
angle proﬁles were cut to give a geometry that match the ribs of the MD Plank.
Due to the fabrication of the angle proﬁles, the dimensions were changed slightly to ease the
production process. The angle proﬁles were thus made as shown in ﬁgure 3.1, with the corre-
sponding values as shown in table 3.1 and 3.2. The dimensioned which the angle was designed
for, is given as ’Design’, while the actual dimensions of the three diﬀerent angles likewise are
given.
It is seen that all the dimensions are made very closely to the speciﬁcations given by the design
dimensions. Most of these dimensions were measure with a calliper, with an exception of the
larger dimensions d2, d3, d5, d6 and da, which were measured with a ruler, which is why there
is no decimal to these. The dimension da represents the moment arm and thus based on the
drilled holes. The largest deviations are from Angle 2A d9 and d10, which are due to the notch

3.1.3
Expected capacity
Due to the slight changes in the dimensions of the angle proﬁles, the expected capacity is also
slightly reduced. However, this has little impact on the objective of the experiments, as it will
still show whether the assumptions about the calculation of the capacity give reasonable values.
In this subsection the calculation of the expected applied failure loads will be described ending
with a presentation of the expected values.
The actual test specimens will have the following cross-section properties:
A = 2914mm2
Iy = 1.658 · 106mm4
(3.1)
The characteristic capacity of the material with alternating direction of pultrusion will be:
fb,k = fb,k,90◦+ fb,k,90◦

(3.2)

of the applied force, F:

Here the 0.28 m are the distance from the line of action of the applied load and the centre of
the angles corner, as illustrated in ﬁgure 3.1. The axial and shear force are based on the 45
degree inclination of the legs of the angle, relative to the direction of the load.
From the section forces, the largest stresses acting at the corner can be determined by:

Here h = 84.5 mm, being the height of the angles cross-section. The determination of the shear

Setting these stresses equal to the capacity and inserting the expressions for the section forces
based on the applied load, the failure load based on the design, characteristic and mean capacity
can be determined. The mean capacity is estimated based on the method presented in section
2.1. These applied failure loads are given in table 3.3, and do not take interaction between the
shear and normal stresses into account. It is found that the normal stress is most critical, and
this is determined assuming the entire normal stress being due to bending. In table 3.3 the ex-

It is expected that all the three individually tested angle proﬁles will be able to withstand a load
higher than that corresponding to the design capacity, as it would otherwise indicate a lower
material strength than that used in general design. Failure loads above that corresponding to
the design capacity are increasingly likely until the failure load corresponding to the estimated
mean capacity is met. This is under the condition that the assumptions for estimating this
value are correct. According to the method used to determine the capacity of the angle proﬁle,
failure loads above that corresponding to the estimated mean capacity will be increasingly
unlikely. However, there might be contributions from the glue within the angle proﬁle, which
are not taken into account, aﬀecting the capacity. Likewise wrong assumption concerning the
alternating direction of pultrusion might results in lower capacities.
The displacements are
expected to be similar to the values given in table 3.3, with a displacement shape as shown in
ﬁgure 3.5, where the corner remains perpendicular.
Figure 3.5: Expected displacement shape of Angle proﬁle, from STAAD.Pro (Exaggerated by
a factor 2 from estimated mean capacity failure load).
==PAGE:202==
Chapter 2. Experimental preparations
11
It is chosen to test the angle proﬁles through tension, which is done in order to avoid stability
problems in the test setup. However, there is the risk of the angle proﬁle failing at the connection
point to the steel fork proﬁle, or failure of the steel fork proﬁle itself. To control that the failure
will happen due to bending of the angle, strength veriﬁcations are performed for both the
connection to the fork proﬁle and for the steel fork proﬁle itself.
The following results are
found, using steel calculations based on [D3]:
• A M20 bolt through the GFRP proﬁle, with 35.5 mm thickness, with alternating direction
of pultrusion, will have a capacity of Fdesign = 60.1kN. This is calculated based on methods
presented in the Main Report.
• The bolt from the fork proﬁle to the test machine is subject to tension, and will have
a capacity of Ft,Rd = 0, 765nAsfub/γM2 = 62.5kN. Here n = 1 is the amount of bolts,
As = 245 mm2 is the stress area of the bolts, fub = 400 MPa is the design tensile strenght
of the bolt and γM2 = 1.2 is the safety coeﬃcient.
• With an underlying washer with a diameter of 40mm, on top of a plate of 10mm, the
punching shear capacity is: Bp,Rd = 0.6nπdmtpfu/γM2 = 84.8kN. Her dm = 40 mm is the
washer diameter and tp = 10 mm is the material thickness, while the rest are as described
for the tensile capacity.
These are considered the likeliest failure modes and it is seen that all these failure modes have
signiﬁcantly more than thrice the design capacity of the angle proﬁle due to bending. It shall be
noted that the M20 bolt going through the angle proﬁle does not meet the minimum distance
requirements from [D4]. It is estimated that this will not be critical, as more than three times the
needed capacity is present, disregarding the minimum distance. The risk is however considered
and strengthening of the connection will be enforced, if failure occurs here in a test specimen.

As seen in table 3.4, it is expected that failure of the angle proﬁle is most critical. The capacity
is for all cases higher than the largest applied force in the frame structure of the Main Report,
which corresponds to an applied load of 19.5 kN. Because the bolts are moved slightly further
from the centre of the connection due to the longer angle proﬁle, the largest bolt force is slightly
lower than in the original design, resulting on a slightly higher capacity based on the direct bolt
contact. However, the increased length of the angle proﬁle increases the moment at the corner of
the angle proﬁle, resulting in failure of the angle becoming more critical. The expected applied
loads are estimated when taking the self-weight of the test specimens and the superimposed
dead load caused by the force distribution proﬁle into account. The contribution from the dead
loads reduces the failure load by 0.3 kN.
For the critical failure loads of the angle proﬁle, the corresponding expected displacements are
given, which are estimated using STAAD.Pro. The deﬂection shape is shown in ﬁgure 3.11,
where the location of the expected displacements are shown with red dots. These two loca-
tions for measuring horizontal and vertical displacements respectively, are found to be the most
interesting. The horizontal displacement at the corner together with another horizontal mea-
surement along the column, will show whether the rigid support is suﬃcient. If this horizontal
displacement is signiﬁcantly larger than the expected value, it indicates that the support in the
test setup has slipped. Having the additional horizontal measurement along the column, will
also allow evaluation of whether the column deformation is linear along the height, indicating
slipping of the support. The vertical displacement at the end shows whether or not the designed
rigid connection acts truly rigid. If the vertical displacement is signiﬁcantly larger than the ex-
pected value, it indicates that the connection only acts semi-rigidly. Apart from this, it will be
of interest to observe how the test specimen acts in the corner, to see whether gaps occur in the
tensile part, shear in the middle and compression in the bottom.

by a factor 10 from estimated mean load).

3.3
Adhesive corner connection
The ﬁnal experiment is the adhesive corner connection, which will be tested using the same test
setup as for the bolted connection. As for the other experiments, a description of the creation
of the test specimens will be given, as well as a presentation of the expected failure load and
location.
3.3.1
Test setup
The test setup is identical to that for the bolted corner connection.
Reference is made to
subsection 3.2.1.
3.3.2
Creating the test specimen
The overall geometry is identical to that of the bolted connection, shown in ﬁgure 3.8. As seen
to the left in ﬁgure 3.12, the angle proﬁles ﬁt very well in one side of the MD Plank, while,
as seen to the right, there is a gap to the other side. As a result, the angle proﬁle can only
eﬀectively be glued to a reduced area of the MD Plank.
Figure 3.12: Picture of the ﬁt of the angle proﬁles within the MD Plank.
The Sikadur330 glue was distributed with a paint roller. This method does not allow a accurate
distribution or even adhesive layer thickness. To control the adhesive layer thickness one could
use high precision glass beads, as was done in [C1]. However, this was not practically possible
here, and would not represent the actual implementation of such an adhesive connection.
When glue was distributed to all inner surfaces of the MD Planks and the corresponding sides
of the angle proﬁles, the angle proﬁles were positioned and MD Planks put together. It was
attempted to avoid sliding the angle proﬁles into place, as this would scrape the glue away.
Instead the angle proﬁles were tilted into place, being possible due to the gap at one side between
==PAGE:208==
Chapter 2. Experimental preparations
17
the positioned angle proﬁle and MD Plank as shown right in ﬁgure 3.12. After positioning all
the angle proﬁles, glue was spread to all the remaining surfaces, as shown in ﬁgure 3.13.
Figure 3.13: Picture of the angle proﬁles in place and glue distributed on the surface.
The glue was likewise distributed to the 6 mm strip proﬁles after which it was carefully placed
on the MD Plank and internal angles. When placed, pressure was applied during the hardening
of the glue. Again, the applied pressure will inﬂuence the thickness of the adhesive layer, while
evening out the distribution. The gluing of the proﬁle was performed 20th of May 2015, and
according to the Datasheet of Sikadur330, the subscribed mechanical properties are obtained
after seven days hardening.
The design was done based on a thickness of the adhesive layer of 2 mm, which, after having
worked practically with the adhesive, is found to be larger than what will be obtained through
the used method. Applying pressure to the connection during hardening, will further decrease
the adhesive layer thickness. In the following subsection the expected capacity will therefore be
based on a reduced adhesive layer of 1 mm.
3.3.3
Expected capacity
The expected failure loads for the adhesive connection are shown in table 3.5. The failure loads
are calculated based on the methods described in the Main Report, and calculated based on
both the presented approaches for the adhesive connection, as well as for failure of the angle
proﬁle. The capacities here are based on the actual geometry of the angle proﬁles, reducing the
adhesive area, and with a adhesive thickness of 1 mm.
It is seen that when using the design capacity, failure will ﬁrst occur in the adhesive layer,
according to approach 1. However, as the adhesive capacity operates with a larger safety coeﬃ-
cient, failure is expected to occur in the angle proﬁle when using the characteristic or estimated
mean capacity. Using approach 2 for determining the capacity of the adhesive connection, the
estimated failure load is much larger than when using approach 1, as already discussed in the

Chapter 4
Conduction of experiment
4.1
Angle proﬁles
The angle proﬁles were tested in an Instron 8521 machine with a capacity of 100 kN. The tests
were performed 29.05.2015 and 01.06.2015. The actual test setup is shown in ﬁgure 4.1, where
the angle proﬁle is seen ﬁxed to the two steel fork proﬁles. The top bridge of the test machine
remains ﬁxed in the set position and the test specimen is pulled from below.
Figure 4.1: Actual test setup for angle proﬁle.
19
==PAGE:211==
Chapter 3. Conduction of experiment
20
The applied load was set to be displacement based. This displacement was measure through the
Instron 8521 machine itself. As a consequence of this, contributions to the displacement from
slipping of the grips on the fork proﬁles, yielding of these fork proﬁles and local deformations
of the holes of the angle proﬁles could occur.
To have a control of the magnitude of this
displacement compared to the actual deformations of the angle proﬁle, a ruler was attached to
the angle proﬁle directly, giving a indication of the magnitude of the displacement contributions.
A more precise setup could be made, using strain gauges and a data logger directly on the test
specimen. However, as the bearing capacity was the main parameter of interest, it was decided
that the used approach was satisfactory. The main test parameters are shown in table 4.1.

of the other angle proﬁles being aﬀected by the yielding, the fork proﬁle was strengthened
through welding after the ﬁrst test. The yielded fork proﬁle is seen left in ﬁgure 4.2, while the

4.2
Current state of bolted corner connection
The preparations for the experiment of the bolted corner connection are close to being com-
pleted, with the test setup almost ready as shown in ﬁgure 4.3. Unfortunately it was not possible
to conduct the experiment before the submission of this report.
Figure 4.3: Current state of the test setup for the bolted corner connection.
Before the experiment can be conduction, a data logger needs to be set up and displacement
traducers placed where the deﬂection are to be measured. As the remaining preparations are
limited, it is expected that the experiment can be conducted before the oral presentation of this
thesis, and that the results can be presented there.
When working on the test setup it was noticed that the beam of the specimen has a signiﬁcant
curvature, as shown left in ﬁgure 4.4.
This is a known problem when using a translucent
polymer matrix, which results in increased shrinkage, which results in deformations if occurring
non-uniformly. This is another challenges which shall be addressed in the further work of the
case structure, but not part of the scope of this thesis. In the conduction of the experiments,
the transversal support will be used to keep the test specimen straight.
Additionally the translucent property of the test specimen was considered for the test specimens.
While viewing the specimens from the sides with no signiﬁcant light variation, the translucent
is hardly visible, though the inherent architecture of the internal ﬁbres can be seen. However,
looking inside the hollow proﬁle, where it is dark, the translucence is clear, as seen right in
ﬁgure 4.4.

Figure 5.1: Plot of results from test of angle proﬁles.

==PAGE:215==
Chapter 4. Test results
24
As mentioned in section 4.1, the displacement is measured by the Instron 8521 testing machine
itself, and is thus the relative displacement of the press.
Based on the measurements from
the ruler attached to the angle proﬁle, superposed to the location of the holes, assuming the
proﬁles remain straight, it is found that the measured displacements of Angle 2 and Angle 3
corresponds well with the actual displacements of the angle proﬁle. This is valid when all initial
measurements are discarded until a consequence load increase occurs, as the measurements until
this displays local deformations around the connection. These ﬁrst measurement were discarded
for all the three tested angles, until a sustained load increase of 0.05 Newton was acquired. For
Angle 3 it is found that the measured displacement around failure is signiﬁcantly overestimated
compared to what can be estimated based on the ruler. This is largely due to the local yielding
of the steel fork proﬁle, mentioned in section 4.1. When examining the plot in ﬁgure 5.1 it is
clearly seen that the yielding occurs at a load of about 5 kN, resulting in a ﬂat plateau, where
the displacement increases but the load remains constant. Generally the curve of Angle 1 is not
as straight as the plots for the other angles.
From the plot in ﬁgure 5.1, it is seen that all the angles were able to sustain a load larger than the
characteristic capacity, while Angle 3 could sustain a signiﬁcantly higher load than the others,
exceeding the estimated mean capacity. All the results are therefore within the expected range,
while the average is slightly below what was expected. The plot is made with crosses, circles or
triangles representing the actual measurement points. Due to the high sample rate, the plots
mainly appear as thick lines. However, immediately after the failure, some measurement points
can be distinguished. This shows a very sudden drop in capacity after failure, as the capacity
is more than halved for all the angles within a few measurements. With a sample rate of 64
Hz, this means that this large reduction of capacity happens within less than a second. This
corresponds to the theory given in the Main Report, stating an elastic material behaviour with
a drastic decrease in stiﬀness after failure occurs. The failure is thus shown to be very brittle,
being a problem when implemented in building, as no warning is given before failure occurs.
Apart from Angle 1, it is seen that the behaviour is almost perfectly elastic until failure.
With a star, square and diamond, in ﬁgure 5.1, the expected displacements are shown corre-
sponding to the expected design, characteristic and estimated mean capacity, as shown in table
3.3. It is seen that the displacements of Angle 2 and Angle 3 correspond very well with these
expected values.
The maximum sustained load for each of the angle proﬁles are shown in table 5.1, together
with the corresponding displacement. Setting the results in increasing order after the maximum
sustained loads, it is noticed that it corresponds with the mass of the angle proﬁles, shown in
table 3.1. Angle 2 is thus the lightest of the proﬁles, and also the one which could sustain
the lowest load, while Angle 3 is the heaviest and the one sustaining the highest load.
As
the measured dimensions are very similar it is not found likely that the small variation that
==PAGE:216==
Chapter 4. Test results
25
exist can have an impact, but the larger mass could indicate a more dense proﬁle and therefore
higher strength. However, the variations could also simply be due to normal variations in the
material. It is seen that the average maximum sustained load is 24.5 kN, being slightly lower
than the estimated load based on the mean capacity of 26.9 kN, as given in table 3.3. The
average displacement is on the other hand signiﬁcantly higher, than that corresponding to the
estimated mean capacity. This is due to the large displacements occurring in Angle 1 due to
the yielding of the steel.

In ﬁgure 5.2, the failure of angle proﬁle 2 is shown. Here it is seen that the failure happens
in the compression loaded part of the angle, which is unexpected, as the normal force in the
angle is tensile, meaning that the stresses in the tensile part of the cross-section will be largest.
The is even more surprising, because the tensile strength according to [D4] is lower than the
compressive perpendicular to the direction of pultrusion, while they are identical parallel to the
direction of pultrusion. Despite this, the failure is clearly compressive, and looks like buckling
of the outer two plates and splitting of the inner plates, resulting in delamination of the GFRP.
This could indicate, that the adhesive connection from the outer plates failed, making these
plates buckle, leaving the remaining cross-section reduced, resulting in the splitting failure.
Apart from being due to compression rather than tension, the failure occurred in the expected
location, where the moment is largest while the cross-section remains unchanged. The same
failure mode occurred in all the angle proﬁles.
Since all the angle proﬁles could sustain loads larger than that corresponding to the characteristic
strength, it is expected that the angle proﬁles in the corner connection likewise will have suﬃcient
capacity to avoid failure at lower loads than expected due to failure of the angle proﬁles.

Chapter 6
Discussion and Conclusions
The experimental work presented in this report unfortunately suﬀers under some of the exper-
iments not being conducted at the time of submission of this thesis. Despite this, experienced
was acquired in preparing the experiments and designing and creating test specimens, and as
the preparation for the remaining experiments are almost complete, it is still expected that they
can be performed in time for the oral presentation, so that the results can be presented then.
During the creation of the test specimens, some changed were made to the design presented in
the Main Report. This was partly a decrease of the cross-section size of the internal angle. This
obviously resulted in a lower strength of the internal angle proﬁles, than that used in the Main
Report. The capacity was calculated based on the actual dimensions of the angle proﬁles, and
the test results showed good compliance with these calculations, both in regards to capacity and
deﬂection. All the capacities of the test specimens were thus above that calculated when using
the characteristic value, while the average was slightly lower than the estimated mean capacity.
It is therefore expected that the calculations done for the angle proﬁles in the Main Report are
valid and are considered veriﬁed by the experiments. As a result of this, it is not expected that
the corner connections will fail below the design strength due to failure of the internal angle
proﬁles. While the failure occurred in the expected location of the angle proﬁles, the failure
was dominated by compression, where it was expected to be a tensile dominated failure, as
the proﬁle was loaded in tension. It was also found that the failure happened very sudden,
resembling a brittle failure, which shall be considered when using the material in buildings.
The bolted connection was made with longer angle proﬁles than in the design in the Main Report,
so that the angles were identical to those used in the adhesive connection. This resulted in a
more spread bolt distribution, reducing the load in each bolt. As a results, the connection is
expected to have suﬃcient capacity to withstand the loads that the rigid frame is designed for,
despite the reduced cross-section of the angle proﬁles.
27
==PAGE:219==
Chapter 5. Discussion and Conclusions
28
The adhesive connection, on the other hand, is expected to have a signiﬁcant reduction of its
capacity due to the slight reduction of the size of the angle proﬁles and a thinner adhesive layer.
This is due to the reduced size of the angle proﬁle, likewise reduces the width of the adhesive
layer, being critical for the capacity. When gluing the adhesive corner connection together,
it was observed that it is not possible to control the adhesive layer thickness, with the used
method, and as a result, it was estimated that the adhesive layer will be thinner than that
used in the design in the Main Report. The adhesive connection made for the test, is thus not
expected to have suﬃcient design capacity to withstand the loads of the rigid frame.
The results that were available from the experimental work has thus shown that the calculated
capacity of the internal angle proﬁle correspond well with the capacity obtained in reality. The
experiments of the bolted and adhesive corner connection are yet to be conducted, to determine
whether the calculations and assumed behaviour of these comply with the experimental results.
